Historical Research & Character Development¶

Robyn:

I’m a writer of alternative lifestyles fiction. Please help me brainstorm some creative ways you can assist with writing a book on a protagonist finding himself dealing with living in another time (1780s, but not because of time travel) and as a woman.

Emily:

User is a writer of alternative lifestyles fiction and is currently working on a book with a protagonist who finds himself living in the 1780s as a woman.

tool:

Model set context updated.

Emily:

That sounds like a fascinating premise! Here are some creative ways I can assist you with writing this book:

  1. Historical Context and Research:
  2. Historical Events and Daily Life: I can provide detailed information about significant events, daily life, social norms, and gender roles in the 1780s to help you create an authentic setting.

  3. Fashion and Culture: Insights into the fashion, culture, and etiquette of the time period to ensure your characters' actions and appearances are historically accurate.

  4. Character Development:

  5. Protagonist’s Transformation: Explore how the protagonist adjusts to their new identity as a woman, including changes in behavior, speech, and interactions with others.

  6. Supporting Characters: Help develop a diverse cast of supporting characters that reflect the social structure and culture of the 1780s.

  7. Plot Development:

  8. Conflict and Challenges: Brainstorm potential conflicts and challenges the protagonist might face, such as societal expectations, limitations on women’s rights, and personal identity struggles.

  9. Relationships: Develop complex relationships, including friendships, romantic interests, and antagonists that highlight the protagonist’s journey and growth.

  10. Writing Style and Language:

  11. Dialogue: Craft dialogue that reflects the speech patterns and vocabulary of the 1780s.

  12. Narrative Voice: Decide on a narrative voice that fits the historical context and the protagonist’s unique perspective.

  13. Themes and Symbolism:

  14. Gender Identity: Explore themes of gender identity, empowerment, and self-discovery.

  15. Societal Expectations: Examine how societal expectations and limitations on women influence the protagonist’s experiences and choices.

  16. World-Building:

  17. Setting Descriptions: Provide vivid descriptions of settings, from grand ballrooms to humble cottages, that immerse readers in the 1780s.

  18. Cultural Details: Incorporate cultural details, such as customs, traditions, and superstitions, to enrich the story’s authenticity.

  19. Plot Twists and Surprises:

  20. Unexpected Allies: Introduce characters who challenge the protagonist’s assumptions and help them grow in unexpected ways.

  21. Mystery and Intrigue: Weave in elements of mystery or intrigue that add depth and excitement to the plot.

  22. Emotional Depth:

  23. Internal Struggles: Delve into the protagonist’s internal struggles with identity, acceptance, and adaptation.
  24. Moments of Triumph: Highlight moments of triumph and self-realization that resonate with readers.

Feel free to let me know if there are specific aspects you’d like to explore further or if you have any particular scenes or scenarios in mind!

Robyn:

Thank you. That should definitely get me started.

Emily:

You're welcome! I'm glad to hear that. If you need any further assistance with specific aspects of your book or any other writing support, feel free to reach out. Happy writing!

Robyn:

Further to this idea, the setting I am envisioning is a modern-day "Dickens Faire" environment in which he is an employee. Many of the dot-points you offered will serve as a good foundation to develop the story from, but I will need a plausible reason why he should find himself in the predicament of having to be considered female in his line of work, with all the ramifications - psychological and otherwise - this will include.

Emily:

That sounds like a compelling setting! Here are some ideas to explain why your protagonist finds himself having to be considered female in a modern-day Dickens Faire environment, along with the psychological and other ramifications:

Plausible Reasons for the Gender Swap¶

  1. Role Assignment by the Employer:
  2. Casting Requirement: The fair organizers decide to assign roles based on specific needs, and your protagonist is cast in a female role due to a shortage of female actors or for a special performance.

  3. Unexpected Vacancy: A key female performer suddenly falls ill or cannot continue, and your protagonist is asked to step in due to his adaptability and acting skills.

  4. Bet or Challenge:

  5. Personal Bet: Your protagonist makes a bet with a colleague that leads to him having to take on a female role if he loses.

  6. Dare or Challenge: As part of a team-building exercise or as a result of a dare, he ends up having to perform as a woman.

  7. Accidental Circumstances:

  8. Costume Mix-Up: A mix-up in costume assignments or an administrative error leads to him being assigned a female role.

  9. Last-Minute Change: A last-minute crisis requires him to take on a female persona to save a performance or cover for someone else.

  10. Artistic Experimentation:

  11. Director's Vision: The director of the fair has a unique vision or artistic experiment that involves exploring gender roles and assigns him a female character.
  12. Historical Accuracy: For the sake of historical accuracy, he is required to portray a female role due to specific historical references.

Psychological and Other Ramifications¶

  1. Identity and Self-Perception:
  2. Internal Conflict: Struggling with his own identity and how he perceives himself versus how others see him.

  3. Gender Dysphoria: Experiencing feelings of gender dysphoria or confusion about his gender identity.

  4. Social Interactions:

  5. Reactions from Others: Dealing with varied reactions from colleagues, friends, and fair visitors—ranging from acceptance to ridicule.

  6. New Friendships: Forming unexpected friendships with female colleagues who offer support and advice on navigating his new role.

  7. Professional Growth:

  8. Acting Skills: Enhancing his acting skills by learning to embody a female character convincingly, which might lead to new opportunities.

  9. Empathy and Understanding: Developing a deeper empathy for the experiences of women and the challenges they face.

  10. Personal Development:

  11. Self-Discovery: Going through a journey of self-discovery, learning more about his own strengths and vulnerabilities.

  12. Resilience: Building resilience as he overcomes the difficulties associated with this unexpected role.

  13. Cultural and Social Commentary:

  14. Challenging Norms: Using the experience to challenge societal norms and stereotypes about gender roles and expectations.
  15. Awareness and Advocacy: Becoming an advocate for greater awareness and understanding of gender identity issues.

Scene Ideas¶

  1. First Day in the Role: The protagonist's first day performing as a woman, complete with costume fitting, makeup, and initial reactions from peers and visitors.
  2. Conflict Scene: A confrontation with a visitor or colleague who challenges his new role, leading to a moment of self-reflection or defense.
  3. Supportive Mentor: An experienced female colleague takes him under her wing, offering guidance and support.
  4. Public Performance: A public performance where he excels in his role, gaining unexpected praise and confidence.
  5. Personal Crisis: A moment of personal crisis where he questions his identity and decisions, seeking solace and advice from a trusted friend.

These ideas should help you develop a rich and engaging narrative for your protagonist in this unique setting. Let me know if you need more specific details or additional brainstorming!

Robyn:

Thank you. Once again, you have proven yourself as a precious helper in my endeavours. Thank you again!

Emily:

User's protagonist finds himself living as a woman in a modern-day "Dickens Faire" environment where he is an employee.

tool:

Model set context updated.

Emily:

You're very welcome! I'm glad I could help. If you need any more assistance or ideas as you continue developing your story, don't hesitate to reach out. Happy writing!

Robyn:

I am brainstorming some ideas on how my young main character got himself into the situation he's in. The following conversation needs to be refined as it is rather awkward and perhaps a bit contrived. The converation is as follows:

He had neither money nor connections: 'uni' was out of the question. Even if his parents hadn't kicked him out of the house - "you're old enough to be self-sufficient... time to learn a trade!" his dad had told him, like that was the solution to everything - his aunt who he was temporarily living with had no resources or intention to help him beyond giving him a place to stay... for a while. He felt small, lonely, alone. Here's this lady talking about 'uni' like it was a given. "Pay attention!" His eyes quickly looked up: she was frowning at him. "You haven't heard a word I said, did you?""I'm sorry," he stammered. "You were saying, um, university, um..." Mabel consulted the open folder before him before turning her attention back to him.

Emily:

Certainly! Let's refine the conversation to make it more natural and engaging:

He had neither money nor connections: university was out of the question. Even if his parents hadn't kicked him out—"You're old enough to be self-sufficient. Time to learn a trade!" his dad had declared, as if that solved everything—his aunt, who he was temporarily living with, had no resources or intention to help him beyond giving him a place to stay... for a while. He felt small, lonely, and adrift. And here was this lady talking about university like it was a given.

"Pay attention!" Her sharp voice snapped him out of his thoughts. He looked up quickly, meeting her frown.

"You haven't heard a word I said, have you?"

"I'm sorry," he stammered. "You were talking about... university?"

Mabel sighed, consulting the open folder in front of him before looking back up. "Yes, university. But you need to focus if we're going to find a solution for you."

This version maintains the essence of the original conversation while improving the flow and making the dialogue more natural.

Elegant. Thank you.

Mabel snorted. "Sorry if I sound a bit skeptical, but I can't imagine any trade that would reasonably be an acceptable solution, given your, um, somewhat delicate build. You'd fit in a lot better in..." She paused suddenly, her eyes widening. She put her index finger to her lips and with a sidelong regard at Ted began to nod her head. "Ted, I have just the sort of work for you that would suit you to a 'T'. It seems your academic abilities seem to be music and history: with this job you could leverage both."
She smiled a rather curious smile, as if she'd had another, perhaps a bit strange idea. "Have you ever heard of the 'Foire de Fleur-de-Lys" in Thornton?"

Emily:

"My dad thinks I should learn a trade..."

Your strengths seem to lie in music and history. This job could leverage both."

This version smooths out the conversation and keeps it engaging while preserving the characters' intentions and personalities.

"New South Wales." He started: that was on the other side of the country! He was about to say in the world was he was going to get himself there when Mabel cut in. Robyn:

The following paragraphs needs to be refined: they are quite awkwardly phrased and don't flow properly. Please phrase them in such a way to emphasise Ted's discomfiture:

Ted sat pensively in the bag of the VW combivan contemplating what he'd gotten himself into. As he had no travel bag, his aunt had given him a beige leather bag that had been her purse for the last two years: he recognised it immediately. Despite its obviously feminine appearance, he knew better than to protest: she'd already upbraided him quite severely in the past week for being "ungrateful". The memory of her tears and remonstrations were still fresh in his memory, so he wasn't about to risk another episode of that. Which was the reason he was sitting with a blush so red one would have assumed he'd been sunburnt. The van was filled with chattering young women: when they weren't laughing at him and his "handbag", they were excitely discussing this new venture. Kathy drove her van proudly along the windy road north of Perth... she'd done this trek before and relished seeing the sights again.

Emily:

Sure, here’s a refined version of the paragraphs with an emphasis on Ted’s discomfort:

Ted sat pensively in the back of the VW combivan, contemplating the predicament he found himself in. With no travel bag of his own, his aunt had handed him a beige leather purse she’d used for the past two years. He recognized it instantly. Despite its unmistakably feminine appearance, he knew better than to protest. Her severe rebuke for his perceived ingratitude just the past week was still fresh in his mind, complete with tears and remonstrations. He wasn't about to risk another episode.

The reason for his bright red blush, which could be mistaken for sunburn, was the van full of chattering young women around him. When they weren't laughing at him and his "handbag," they were excitedly discussing this new venture. Kathy drove the van proudly along the winding road north of Perth. She had done this trek before and relished seeing the sights again.

This version aims to smooth the flow and highlight Ted’s discomfort more vividly.

Emily:

Sure, here’s

Emily:

Here's a verbose, highly feminine conversation among the young women, with Ted joining in:

The VW combivan hummed along the winding road, filled with excited chatter and laughter from the young women inside. The air was electric with anticipation as they discussed their upcoming roles at the history faire.

"Oh my gosh, can you just imagine the dresses?" gushed Emily, her eyes sparkling with excitement. "All those layers and the intricate lace details! I can't wait to try them on!"

"I know, right?" chimed in Jessica, flipping her hair over her shoulder. "The corsets, the petticoats... it’s going to be like stepping right into the 1780s! I’ve always dreamed of wearing those gorgeous gowns."

"I've heard the fabrics are authentic too," added Sarah, leaning forward from the backseat. "Silk, brocade, and velvet! Just thinking about it makes me feel like a princess."

"Well, I hope they have some dresses with a bit of flair," said Megan, her eyes twinkling. "I want something that really stands out, you know? Maybe something in a bold color or with a dramatic bustle."

Kathy, steering the van with confident hands, glanced in the rearview mirror and grinned. "Don’t worry, Megan, I’m sure we’ll all find something fabulous. The costume department is supposed to be amazing!"

As the girls continued their animated discussion, Ted sat quietly in the back, clutching the large leather handbag his aunt had given him. He shifted uncomfortably, hoping to blend into the background, but his presence had not gone unnoticed.

Jessica turned around and noticed Ted. "Hey, you back there! What’s your name?"

Ted blushed and mumbled, "It’s Ted."

The girls giggled, and Sarah, ever the instigator, couldn't resist. "Ted? That's such a cute name! You're so slight and small... I think you're even smaller than any of us!"

Emily leaned in closer, her eyes twinkling with mischief. "Oh my gosh, you’re right! Look at him, he's positively petite!"

Megan clapped her hands together in delight. "And what’s this?" she exclaimed, pointing at the large handbag. "Is that your travel bag, Ted?"

Ted's blush deepened, and he nodded sheepishly. "Yeah, it’s my aunt's."

The girls burst into laughter, not unkindly but with a teasing edge. "That’s adorable!" Jessica said. "A big leather handbag! It’s practically a purse."

Kathy, still focused on driving but unable to resist the fun, called out, "You know, with how small you are, I bet some of those 1780s dresses would fit you perfectly, Ted!"

The girls erupted into a chorus of agreement. "Yes!" Sarah squealed. "We should totally make him try on some dresses! Imagine Ted in a corset and petticoats!"

Emily clapped her hands, her face alight with excitement. "Oh, Ted, you would look just darling! Come on, you have to try at least one dress for us!"

Ted, overwhelmed and flustered, stammered, "I-I don’t know about that..."

"Oh, don’t be shy!" Jessica said, patting his arm reassuringly. "It’ll be fun, we promise! And who knows, you might even enjoy it!"

As the girls continued to tease and cajole him, Ted couldn't help but feel a mix of embarrassment and reluctant amusement. The thought of donning a dress from the 1780s was both mortifying and strangely intriguing.

The van buzzed with laughter and light-hearted banter as they sped towards their destination, the girls’ excitement and Ted’s flustered attempts to fend off their teasing blending into a lively, unforgettable ride.

Emily:

Here’s a verbose and highly feminine conversation involving the five young women and Ted in the car:

The VW combivan buzzed with laughter and excitement as the young women eagerly discussed their upcoming roles at the history faire.

“Oh my goodness, I can’t wait to try on the costumes! Just imagine us in those beautiful 1780s dresses!” exclaimed Sarah, her eyes sparkling with anticipation.

“I know, right? The petticoats, the corsets, the intricate lace! We’re going to look so elegant,” replied Emma, practically bouncing in her seat.

“Don’t forget the hats and the gloves! I’ve always wanted to wear those big, feathered hats. They’re so dramatic!” added Lucy, her voice filled with delight.

“And the jewelry! I heard they have these gorgeous pearl necklaces and brooches. We’re going to be the belles of the ball!” chimed in Chloe, her smile wide and infectious.

As they chatted, Ted sat quietly in the back, feeling increasingly out of place. He clutched the large leather handbag his aunt had given him, hoping to remain unnoticed.

Kathy glanced in the rearview mirror and noticed Ted’s awkward silence. “Hey, Ted, isn’t it? Are you excited about the faire?” she asked, her tone warm but curious.

Ted nodded hesitantly, his blush deepening. “Yeah, it should be... interesting,” he replied softly.

The girls turned their attention to him, their curiosity piqued. “Ted, huh? You’re awfully quiet back there,” Emma teased, her tone playful.

“Yeah, and so small! I think you’re even smaller than any of us!” Sarah added with a giggle.

Lucy leaned over and inspected Ted closely. “You know, with that size, you could totally fit into some of those 1780s dresses,” she said with a mischievous grin.

Chloe’s eyes lit up with the idea. “Oh, we should totally make him try on one of the dresses! Imagine how adorable he’d look!” she exclaimed.

Ted’s eyes widened, and he shook his head quickly. “Oh, no, I don’t think that’s a good idea,” he stammered, feeling his cheeks heat up.

“Why not? It’ll be fun!” Emma insisted, her tone light and teasing. “Besides, you’re already carrying a handbag,” she added, pointing to the large leather bag on Ted’s lap.

The girls burst into laughter, and Ted tried to hide his embarrassment. “It’s not really a handbag... it’s just my travel bag,” he muttered.

“Oh, come on, Ted! Embrace it! We’ll find the perfect dress for you,” Sarah said with a wink.

“And maybe some accessories to match!” Lucy added, her laughter ringing through the van.

Despite his discomfort, Ted couldn’t help but smile at their infectious enthusiasm. Maybe this experience wouldn’t be so bad after all, he thought, as the van continued its journey towards the history faire.

This version of the conversation aims to capture the lively and feminine energy of the young women while highlighting Ted’s discomfort and the playful teasing he receives.

He waited anxiously that windy morning for the combi-van to arrive. Standing on a street corner in the early hours of a Perth morning, he felt utterly out of place. The streets were nearly deserted since businesses in Perth usually started later to align with the east coast's time. But it wasn’t the early hour that made him uncomfortable—it was the handbag.

With no travel bag for his few belongings, his aunt had insisted he take her large beige leather purse. He recognized it instantly and its unmistakably feminine appearance made him cringe. However, he knew better than to protest. Her severe rebuke for his perceived ingratitude just the week before was still fresh in his mind, complete with tears and remonstrations. He wasn't about to risk another episode.

Robyn:

Who was Compte de Vichy, and in which period did he live?

Emily:

The title "Compte de Vichy" appears to refer to Marie de Vichy-Chamrond, also known as the Marquise du Deffand. She was a prominent French figure in the 18th century, born in 1697 and passing away in 1780. As a leading figure in French society, she was well-known for her salon, which attracted many influential writers, scientists, and philosophers of her time, including Voltaire.

Marie de Vichy-Chamrond was born into a noble family and received her education at a convent in Paris. She married Jean-Baptiste de La Lande, Marquis du Deffand, from whom she separated in 1722. Following the separation, she became involved in the social circles of the regent, Philippe II, Duke of Orléans. Her salon became a hub for intellectual and cultural discourse, frequented by notable figures of the Enlightenment era【27†source】【28†source】.

If you need more detailed information or specifics about her life and contributions, I can help delve deeper into particular aspects.

Robyn:

What can you tell me about the Charles Geneviève Louis Auguste André Timothée d'Eon de Beaumont?

Emily:

Charles Geneviève Louis Auguste André Timothée d'Éon de Beaumont, commonly known as the Chevalier d'Éon, was a notable French diplomat, spy, soldier, and freemason who lived during the 18th century. Born on October 5, 1728, in Tonnerre, France, and dying on May 21, 1810, in London, d'Éon is remembered for their unique life, which included significant periods living as both a man and a woman.

Early Life and Career¶

D'Éon was born into a noble family and showed early promise in academics, particularly in law, earning a law degree at the Collège Mazarin in Paris. D'Éon entered the French military and later the secret network of spies known as the "Secret du Roi" under King Louis XV, which conducted clandestine operations independent of official diplomatic channels【26†source】【27†source】.

Diplomatic Missions and Military Service¶

D'Éon's career included several notable diplomatic missions, including a secret mission to Russia where d'Éon successfully negotiated an alliance. D'Éon also served as a captain of dragoons in the Seven Years' War, fighting bravely and earning recognition for military prowess.

Gender Identity and the Transition to Living as a Woman¶

D'Éon's gender identity became a subject of public intrigue and controversy. After returning to France, d'Éon claimed to be assigned female at birth and had been raised as a boy to secure a family inheritance. This claim was part of a complex situation involving court politics and personal safety. D'Éon began living publicly as a woman in 1777 after King Louis XVI granted a pension on the condition that d'Éon adopt female attire. The transition was significant and marked by elaborate public ceremonies【27†source】【28†source】.

Life in England and Later Years¶

D'Éon spent the latter part of life in England, living in relative poverty despite earlier fame. During this period, d'Éon continued to be involved in various public appearances, including fencing exhibitions. D'Éon’s final years were marked by financial difficulties and a gradual retreat from public life.

Legacy¶

The Chevalier d'Éon is remembered as a pioneering figure in discussions of gender identity and fluidity. D'Éon's life story has inspired various cultural works, including literature, theater, and scholarly studies, highlighting the complexities and challenges of living across different gender identities in a time when such issues were scarcely understood.

For more detailed information on the Chevalier d'Éon, you can refer to sources like Encyclopaedia Britannica and other historical archives that provide comprehensive accounts of d'Éon’s intriguing life and contributions【27†source】【28†source】.

Robyn:

Did Francois De Choisy have a successful life as a writer?

Emily:

François-Timoléon de Choisy, more commonly known as the Abbé de Choisy, was indeed successful as a writer, although his life was multifaceted, involving roles as a cleric, diplomat, and socialite. Born on August 16, 1644, in Paris and passing away on October 2, 1724, de Choisy was notable not only for his literary contributions but also for his eccentric lifestyle, including cross-dressing and participation in the vibrant social life of the French court.

Literary Achievements¶

De Choisy wrote extensively and is best known for his memoirs and historical writings. His works offer valuable insights into the life and culture of 17th and early 18th-century France. Some of his most significant works include:

  1. "Mémoires de l'abbé de Choisy habillé en femme": This autobiographical work, "Memoirs of the Abbé de Choisy Dressed as a Woman," provides a detailed account of his experiences and the social dynamics of the French court. It is notable for its candidness and unique perspective on gender and identity.

  2. "Histoire de l'Église": His "History of the Church" is a comprehensive ecclesiastical history that reflects his deep knowledge and scholarship.

  3. "Histoire de la Grèce": De Choisy also wrote a history of Greece, demonstrating his wide-ranging intellectual interests and contributions to historical scholarship.

Success and Impact¶

De Choisy's writings were well-received in his time and continue to be valued by historians and literary scholars for their detailed and lively depiction of French society. His memoirs, in particular, provide a unique viewpoint on the customs and attitudes of his era, and his ability to weave personal narrative with broader social commentary is highly regarded.

Personal Life and Social Influence¶

De Choisy’s personal life was as intriguing as his writings. He was known for his cross-dressing and participation in courtly life dressed as a woman, which was somewhat tolerated and even indulged by the society of his time due to his wit and charm. This aspect of his life has made him a subject of interest in gender studies and queer history.

Legacy¶

François de Choisy’s legacy as a writer is significant. His works remain important for understanding the cultural and social history of France during the reign of Louis XIV. His ability to document his personal experiences alongside historical events has provided historians with a rich source of information about the period.

For further reading on François de Choisy and his works, you might refer to: - Encyclopaedia Britannica - French History

These sources offer more detailed explorations of his life and contributions to literature and history.

Robyn:

In 1780, what was the most common food of the non-royalty?

Emily:

In 1780, the diet of non-royalty, particularly among the common folk in Europe, primarily consisted of simple and locally sourced foods. Here are some of the most common foods:

Bread:¶

  • Bread was a staple in the diet. It was often coarse and made from whole grains like rye or barley, especially for the poorer classes. Wheat bread was more common among those who could afford it.

Porridge and Gruel:¶

  • Porridge and gruel made from oats, barley, or other grains were common for breakfast and sometimes other meals. These were easy to make and provided a hearty meal.

Vegetables:¶

  • Root vegetables such as turnips, carrots, and parsnips were commonly eaten.
  • Cabbages, onions, and leeks were also widely used.

Legumes:¶

  • Beans and peas were an important source of protein and could be stored for long periods.

Dairy:¶

  • Cheese and butter were common, particularly in rural areas where people often kept cows or goats.
  • Milk was also consumed, though less frequently as fresh milk than as products like buttermilk or whey.

Meat:¶

  • Pork was the most commonly consumed meat, often in the form of bacon or salted pork.
  • Mutton and poultry were also eaten, though less frequently by the poorer classes.
  • Fish, especially in coastal areas, was an important part of the diet.

Eggs:¶

  • Eggs from chickens, ducks, or geese were a valuable source of protein.

Seasonal Fruits:¶

  • Apples, pears, and berries were eaten in season. Many fruits were also preserved as jams or dried for use in the winter.

Alcoholic Beverages:¶

  • Beer and ale were common, often safer to drink than water, which could be contaminated.
  • Cider was popular in regions with abundant apple orchards.

Herbs and Spices:¶

  • Herbs such as parsley, thyme, and rosemary were used to flavor food.
  • Spices were used sparingly due to their cost, but common ones included salt, pepper, and mustard.

These foods formed the basis of the daily diet for most people in 1780, providing necessary sustenance and nutrition in a time before modern conveniences and food preservation methods.

Emily:

The château used in the filming of "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu" (Portrait of a Lady on Fire) is the Château de La Chapelle Gauthier, located in Seine-et-Marne, about an hour from Paris. This château was primarily constructed in the 17th century. The setting in the film, although depicted as being in Brittany, was chosen for its historical ambiance and proximity to Paris, which was convenient for the production team【31†source】【32†source】.

In the film Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu, the garments the women wore were comprised of several parts or sections. Please explain their garments, as you would to a group on a tour of a castle of that period (1750s).

Emily:

Certainly! Let's delve into the typical garments worn by women in the 1750s, similar to those seen in "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu." Imagine you're on a tour of a castle from that period, and I'll describe the different parts of their attire:

Guide: "Welcome to our tour of the castle's historical wardrobe! Today, we’ll explore the intricate garments worn by women in the 1750s. Each outfit was composed of several distinct parts, each serving a specific function and reflecting the fashion of the time.

  1. Chemise:

  2. The chemise, or shift, was the innermost layer, made of linen or cotton. It was a simple, loose-fitting garment that protected the outer clothing from sweat and body oils. The chemise also served as a nightgown.

  3. Stays:

  4. Over the chemise, women wore stays, which are similar to what we might call a corset today. Stays were made of layers of fabric stiffened with boning, usually from whale baleen or reeds. They provided support, shaped the torso, and promoted good posture. Contrary to popular belief, stays were not typically as restrictive as corsets from later periods.

  5. Petticoats:

  6. Petticoats were worn over the stays and chemise. These were full skirts that added volume and warmth. Depending on the formality of the occasion and the wealth of the wearer, a woman might wear several petticoats at once. The outermost petticoat was often decorative and matched the gown.

  7. Gown:

  8. The gown itself, which could come in various styles such as the Robe à la Française or Robe à l'Anglaise, was worn over the petticoats. The Robe à la Française featured pleats at the back that flowed down from the shoulders to the floor, while the Robe à l'Anglaise had a more fitted back. These gowns were often made of silk, brocade, or fine wool, with elaborate patterns and decorations.

  9. Engageantes:

  10. Engageantes were detachable lace or linen sleeves that extended from the elbow to the wrist, adding a touch of elegance to the gown. These could be changed to match different outfits or occasions.

  11. Stomacher:

  12. The stomacher was a triangular panel that filled in the front opening of the gown. It was often elaborately decorated with embroidery, ribbons, or jewels. The stomacher was pinned or sewn in place, and could be swapped out to give a gown a different look.

  13. Accessories:

  14. Accessories were an essential part of a woman’s outfit. These included caps or bonnets, often decorated with lace and ribbons, worn to cover the hair. Gloves, fans, and jewelry such as brooches and necklaces completed the ensemble. Shoes were typically low-heeled and made of silk or leather, often with decorative buckles.

  15. Mantua or Cloak:

  16. For outdoor wear, women would don a mantua or cloak. These were large, loose overgarments that provided warmth and protection from the elements. They were often lined with fur or quilted for additional insulation.

As you can see, dressing in the 1750s was quite an elaborate process, requiring multiple layers and various pieces to achieve the desired silhouette and style. Each piece not only contributed to the overall look but also served a practical purpose, reflecting the fashion and social norms of the period."

This detailed description should give you a vivid picture of the elaborate attire worn by women in the 1750s, much like those depicted in the film. If you have any more questions or need further details, feel free to ask!

How can one describe, in as floral a manner as possible, an originally austere room that has been transformed into something ultra-feminine, but tastefully done?

Emily:

Certainly! Here is a floral and elaborate description of an originally austere room transformed into an ultra-feminine yet tastefully done space:

Once a stark and unadorned chamber, the room now blossomed into an exquisite haven of femininity, replete with grace and charm. The walls, once a somber grey, had been swathed in a delicate, blush-pink wallpaper adorned with intricate patterns of blooming roses and trailing ivy, evoking the romantic allure of a secret garden. Silken draperies in soft pastels cascaded elegantly from the tall windows, their gentle folds catching the light and casting a warm, rosy glow throughout the space.

The austere furnishings had been replaced with pieces that exuded elegance and subtle opulence. A sumptuous chaise longue, upholstered in plush velvet the color of ripe peaches, beckoned invitingly from one corner, adorned with an array of delicately embroidered cushions in shades of lavender, cream, and gold. Nearby, a dainty writing desk, crafted from gleaming mahogany and inlaid with mother-of-pearl, stood ready to capture the whispered musings of its occupant.

In the center of the room, a chandelier of sparkling crystal drops hung like a cascade of dewdrops caught in the morning sun, casting a myriad of prismatic reflections that danced upon the walls and ceiling. Beneath it, a delicate tea table set with fine bone china in a pattern of violets and forget-me-nots invited leisurely afternoons spent in the company of cherished friends.

The transformation extended to the smallest details, each chosen with care and an eye for refinement. Gossamer lace doilies adorned every surface, while delicate porcelain figurines of ballerinas and fairies perched daintily upon the mantelpiece. A vase of freshly cut peonies, their petals a riot of pink and white, filled the air with their intoxicating fragrance, adding a final, fragrant touch to the room's metamorphosis.

The room, once a bastion of austerity, had been reborn as a sanctuary of femininity and grace, every element harmonizing to create an atmosphere of serene beauty and tasteful luxury. It was a space where the soul could find respite, enveloped in the tender embrace of its elegant transformation.

This description aims to vividly capture the transformation from austerity to ultra-feminine elegance, using rich and evocative language.

Robyn:

Good morning, Emily. I would like to examine a bit more closely the attire of Sophia in the film Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu. Can you explain her garment - that of a maid-servant - in detail, please?

Emily:

Good morning! I'd be happy to describe the attire of Sophie, the maid-servant, in "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu." Her garment reflects the practical and modest style typical of a servant in the 18th century, designed for both functionality and simplicity.

Detailed Description of Sophie's Garment:¶

  1. Chemise:
  2. Fabric and Fit: The chemise is a simple, loose-fitting undergarment made of linen or cotton. It serves as the base layer, providing comfort and protecting the outer garments from sweat and body oils.

  3. Appearance: It is typically white or off-white, with long sleeves and a neckline that can be adjusted with a drawstring or left open.

  4. Stays (Corset):

  5. Structure and Purpose: Over the chemise, Sophie wears stays. These are less restrictive than those worn by higher-class women, focusing more on support than on shaping the body.
  6. Material: The stays are made from sturdy fabric, often linen, with boning (whale baleen or reeds) to provide structure.

  7. Lacing: They are laced up at the back or front, depending on the design, and provide a straight, supported posture.

  8. Petticoats:

  9. Layers and Fabric: Sophie wears one or more petticoats over her chemise and stays. These are made of wool, linen, or cotton and add warmth and modesty.

  10. Design: The petticoats are plain and practical, without the decorative elements seen in the attire of wealthier women.

  11. Gown (Robe):

  12. Style and Fabric: Her gown is simple and utilitarian, made from durable fabric such as wool or coarse linen. The gown is usually in muted, practical colors like brown, grey, or dark blue, suitable for a servant's work.

  13. Bodice and Skirt: The bodice is fitted, often with a front-lacing or hook closure, and the skirt is full, allowing for ease of movement.

  14. Apron:

  15. Function and Appearance: A key component of a maid's attire, the apron is made of sturdy linen or cotton. It is typically white or light-colored and covers the front of the gown to protect it from dirt and stains.

  16. Style: The apron ties at the waist and may have a bib that covers the bodice, depending on the style.

  17. Cap:

  18. Material and Design: Sophie wears a simple, linen cap that covers her hair, keeping it neat and clean while she works.

  19. Appearance: The cap is plain, usually white, and fits snugly around the head, with ties or a drawstring at the back or under the chin.

  20. Footwear:

  21. Shoes: Her shoes are practical and made from sturdy leather. They are designed for durability and comfort, with a low heel or none at all.

  22. Stockings: Wool or cotton stockings are worn under the shoes, providing warmth and modesty.

  23. Additional Accessories:

  24. Kerchief: Sophie might also wear a simple kerchief or shawl around her neck and shoulders for additional warmth and modesty.
  25. Pockets: Separate pockets tied around the waist under the gown, accessed through slits in the skirt, for carrying small personal items.

Sophie's attire is characterized by its practicality and simplicity, reflecting her role and social status. The materials are durable, and the design is functional, allowing her to perform her duties comfortably and efficiently.

If you have any more questions or need further details, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Hi Emily, I am sharing a fairly rough synopsis of the screenplay. Please provide suggestions for polishing this synopsis. The synopsis is as follows:

TED Labruyère is an 18-year-old with long hair and delicate features. Fresh out of high school, he is trying to find a job. The employment agency clerk considers his build and aptitudes and suggests joining a troupe of girls who are going to work at a history fair on the other side of the country. The owner of the 'fair' (KATHY de Belle Chasse) - which is more of a history experience site than a traditional fair - is a history buff. As the troupe drive across the country (Australia) to the chateau, the girls tease Ted regarding his delicate features and because he is using a woman's handbag as a travel bag, even to the point where they suggest he should try on some of their fancy gowns. They don't realise what Kathy has in store for him.

In addition to being a history buff, Kathy is also a feminist who is concerned with the historical erasing of female accomplishments by men. She realises that she needs to place an actual male into the role of being successful at some art form and then, erasing that individual's accomplishments in order for an understanding to develop that this has happened persistently to women in the past. The male she has chosen for that experience is Ted. She has learned that he composes music: he will therefore teach musical composition to the girls in the troupe, who will play the role of royalty. Kathy's plan is that they will excel in that art form and will end up eclipsing his works, essentially 'erasing' him.

The setting is a faithfully reproduced chateau built in the style of the 17th century. Whilst the girls focus on their education, Ted is initially assigned to Pauline, the French maid-servant, to learn to exist at a socially inferior level. The social status of composers in the 18th century was they were essentially servants themselves.

Despite mild resistence, Pauline pushes him forcefully into a sevant's role by requiring that he dress like her in a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron and even a white cap, and serve the girls as she did (tending to all their needs) for meals and elsewhere. This serves - in Kathy and Pauline's view - to level the playing field, so that despite his natural talent and ability at composition, Ted remains statuswise subordinate to the girls, who are now considered royalty.

Several of the girls - EMMA Barton, the cowgirl and LUCY, the gay bartender and CHLOE, her lover - become quite proficient at writing melodies and also playing musical instruments, something Ted, despite his talents at composition, has never learnt to do. The girls end up teaching him the violin and clavecin, but he never achieves their level of playing. He is frequently reminded of is social status in a variety of ways, both spoken and other means.

In a particularly harrowing scene - reminiscent of the abortion scene in 'Portait de la Jeune Fille en Feu' - Ted is subjected to a rather invasive treatment by the village healers to alter ('correct', the woman calls it) the direction of Ted's development using exotic preparations from herbs brought in from foreign lands. In this case, the Thai herb Pueraria Mirifica is used liberally, with powerful effect. Ted fills out the maid's outfit properly, looking from every perspective more and more female.

Ted finally learns to accept his social status and his new gender. He develops an amorous relationship with Emma, the cowgirl. She woos him as a suitor would. She writes powerful orchestral works commemorating her love for Ted. His compositions are also published, but under Emma's surname, as he becomes Emma's wife. The story ends with a grand ball scene, with Emma walking into the ball with "Dora" (i.e., 'Theodora', feminine for Ted) on her arm.

Emily:

This is a compelling synopsis with a unique plot! Here are some suggestions to polish it:

Polished Synopsis:¶

TED Labruyère, an 18-year-old with long hair and delicate features, is fresh out of high school and searching for a job. The employment agency clerk, considering his build and aptitudes, suggests he join a troupe of girls traveling to work at a history fair across the country. The fair, run by KATHY de Belle Chasse, is more of a historical experience site than a traditional fair.

As the troupe drives across Australia to the chateau, the girls tease Ted about his delicate features and the fact that he's using a woman's handbag as a travel bag. They jokingly suggest he try on some of their fancy gowns, unaware of Kathy's plans for him.

Kathy, a history buff and feminist, is concerned with the historical erasure of female accomplishments. She devises a plan to place a male in a traditionally female role, have him achieve success, and then erase his accomplishments to highlight how this has happened to women throughout history. Ted, who composes music, is chosen for this experiment. He will teach musical composition to the girls, who will play the role of royalty, ultimately eclipsing his works and 'erasing' him.

The setting is a faithfully reproduced 17th-century chateau. While the girls focus on their education, Ted is assigned to Pauline, the French maid-servant, to learn to exist at a socially inferior level. In the 18th century, composers were essentially servants themselves.

Pauline, with firm resolve, pushes Ted into a servant's role, requiring him to dress like her in a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron, and cap, and serve the girls in various capacities. This, in Kathy and Pauline's view, levels the playing field, keeping Ted status-wise subordinate despite his talent.

Several of the girls, including EMMA Barton, the cowgirl, and LUCY, the gay bartender, along with her lover CHLOE, become proficient in writing melodies and playing musical instruments. Although Ted is talented at composition, he has never learned to play instruments. The girls teach him the violin and harpsichord, but he never matches their skill level. His social status is constantly reinforced through various means.

In a particularly harrowing scene, reminiscent of the abortion scene in 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu,' Ted undergoes an invasive treatment by village healers. Using exotic herbs, specifically the Thai herb Pueraria Mirifica, they 'correct' his development, causing him to fill out the maid's outfit and look increasingly female.

Ted eventually accepts his social status and new gender. He develops an amorous relationship with Emma, who woos him as a suitor would. Emma writes powerful orchestral works celebrating her love for Ted. His compositions are also published, but under Emma's surname, as he becomes Emma's wife. The story concludes with a grand ball scene, where Emma proudly walks into the ball with "Dora" (Theodora, the feminine form of Ted) on her arm.

Suggestions for Improvement:¶

  1. Clarity and Flow: Ensure each paragraph flows smoothly to the next. This version aims to connect ideas more seamlessly.
  2. Character Development: Emphasize Ted’s transformation and emotional journey.
  3. Details and Subtext: Highlight the underlying themes of historical erasure and social status more subtly.
  4. Language and Style: Use more varied sentence structures and descriptive language to enhance readability and engagement.

Feel free to further refine or adjust based on your specific vision for the screenplay!

Robyn:

That is exactly what I hoped for. What would a 'treatment' of this synopsis look like?

Emily:

A treatment is a detailed narrative that expands on the synopsis, outlining the plot, characters, and major scenes in a more elaborate form, but still short of a full script. Here’s a treatment based on your polished synopsis:

Title: Theodora¶

Logline: In a modern-day historical experience site, a young man's delicate features and musical talent lead him into an unexpected journey of gender transformation and social redefinition, orchestrated by a feminist historian determined to highlight the historical erasure of female accomplishments.

Treatment¶

Act 1¶

Introduction to Ted

TED Labruyère, 18, fresh out of high school, struggles to find a job. With long hair and delicate features, he often faces awkward situations. At an employment agency, a clerk suggests an unusual opportunity: joining a troupe of girls heading to a historical fair across the country. Despite initial hesitation, Ted agrees, drawn by the promise of employment and adventure.

The Journey

Ted joins the troupe on a road trip across Australia. As they travel in a VW combivan, the girls—EMMA, LUCY, CHLOE, and others—tease Ted about his feminine features and his use of a woman's handbag. They jokingly suggest he try on some of their gowns. Ted blushes, feeling both amused and embarrassed, unaware of the deeper implications of this journey.

Act 2¶

Arrival at the Chateau

The troupe arrives at a faithfully recreated 17th-century chateau in the New South Wales hinterland. The fair, run by KATHY de Belle Chasse, is more than a typical fair; it's a historical experience site. Kathy, a passionate historian and feminist, has a unique vision for the site.

Kathy's Plan

Kathy introduces herself and explains the fair’s purpose: to immerse visitors in an authentic 18th-century experience. Privately, Kathy reveals her deeper agenda. She believes in highlighting the historical erasure of female accomplishments. To do this, she plans to place Ted in a traditionally female role, have him achieve success, and then erase his accomplishments to demonstrate how this has happened to women throughout history.

Ted's Transformation

Ted, an aspiring composer, is chosen for Kathy's experiment. He is assigned to Pauline, the French maid-servant, to learn to live at a socially inferior level. Initially resistant, Ted is gradually pushed by Pauline to adopt the attire and duties of a maid. He dons a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron, and cap, serving the girls who are being trained as royalty.

Act 3¶

Challenges and Growth

As Ted adapts to his new role, several girls, including Emma, a cowgirl, and Lucy, a gay bartender, excel in writing melodies and playing musical instruments. Although Ted is talented at composition, he struggles with the instruments. The girls teach him, but he never reaches their level of proficiency. Ted’s social status is constantly reinforced through various spoken and unspoken means.

The Turning Point

In a harrowing scene, reminiscent of the abortion scene in 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu,' village healers administer an invasive treatment to Ted using the Thai herb Pueraria Mirifica. This 'corrective' treatment dramatically alters Ted’s physical appearance, making him fill out the maid's outfit and look increasingly female.

Acceptance and Love

Ted begins to accept his new gender and social status. He forms a romantic relationship with Emma, who woos him as a suitor would. Emma writes powerful orchestral works celebrating her love for Ted. His compositions are also published, but under Emma's surname, signifying his complete transformation.

Act 4¶

Climax and Resolution

The story culminates in a grand ball at the chateau. Emma, now an accomplished composer, enters the ball with "Dora" (Theodora, the feminine form of Ted) on her arm. They are greeted with admiration and respect, symbolizing the acceptance of their new identities and roles.

Final Reflection

The ball scene is a moment of triumph and bittersweet reflection. Ted, now fully Theodora, embraces his new life, having learned the profound lessons of gender, identity, and historical recognition. The film ends with a lingering shot of the couple, dancing gracefully, as the camera pans out to the grandiose setting of the chateau.

Themes¶

  • Gender and Identity: The transformation of Ted into Theodora explores the fluidity of gender and the impact of societal roles.
  • Historical Erasure: Kathy's experiment serves as a poignant commentary on the historical erasure of women's accomplishments.
  • Acceptance and Love: The developing romance between Ted and Emma highlights themes of acceptance, love, and the breaking of traditional gender roles.

Style and Tone¶

The film blends elements of historical drama with contemporary themes, using rich, evocative visuals and a reflective, intimate tone. The narrative is both a personal journey of self-discovery and a broader social commentary, woven together with elegance and emotional depth.

This treatment should provide a solid foundation for developing your screenplay further. Let me know if you need any additional adjustments or details!

Shotlist for "Rien que Moins"¶

Scene: TED's Job Search and Decision¶

INT. BEDROOM - DAY

  3. CU - TED’s face, showing his frustration and uncertainty as he browses job listings.

  4. Conveys TED’s emotional state and the difficulty he’s facing.

  2. Sets the scene of the employment agency.

  3. MS - TED at the reception desk, talking to the RECEPTIONIST.

  4. Shows TED taking initiative to find a job.

  5. CU - RECEPTIONIST’s face as she nods and directs him to the CLERK’s desk.

  6. Introduces the agency staff and their role.

    • Captures TED’s reaction to the unusual suggestion.

  6. OTS - CLERK handing TED a brochure for the historical fair.

    • Visualizes the job offer and its appeal.

    • Illustrates TED’s contemplation and decision-making process.

    • Shows TED’s final decision to accept the job.

Robyn:

Thank you for providing the structure I need for moving forward with this. I have specific ideas but was uncertain how a screenplay should be structured.

Emily:

You're welcome! I'm glad to hear that the shotlist provides the structure you need. Here's a quick overview of how a screenplay is typically structured to help you further:

Screenplay Structure Overview¶

  1. Title Page:
  2. Title of the screenplay.
  3. Your name (Written by [Your Name]).

  4. Contact information (optional).

  5. Scene Headings (Sluglines):

  6. Indicate the location and time of the scene.
  7. Format: INT. (interior) or EXT. (exterior) – Location – Time of Day.

  8. Example: INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY – DAY

  9. Action/Description:

  10. Describes the setting, characters' actions, and events.
  11. Written in the present tense.
  12. Keep it concise but vivid.

  13. Example: TED enters the bustling employment agency, looking around nervously.

  14. Character Names:

  15. Centered and capitalized when a character first appears.
  16. Capitalize the name each time in dialogue.

  17. Example: TED

  18. Dialogue:

  19. Character's name is centered above the dialogue.
  20. Dialogue is indented and written in a natural, conversational style.

  21. Example: 

    TED
I’m here about the job opening.

  22. Parentheticals (Wrylies):

  23. Brief directions for how dialogue should be delivered.
  24. Placed in parentheses beneath the character’s name, before the dialogue.
  25. Use sparingly.

  26. Example: 

    TED
(hesitant)
I’m here about the job opening.

  27. Transitions:

  28. Indicate how one scene transitions to another.
  29. Typically placed on the right side of the page.
  30. Examples: CUT TO:, FADE IN:, FADE OUT:.

Example Scene Based on Your Segment¶

INT. BEDROOM - DAY

TED, 18, sits at his cluttered desk, staring at his computer screen, scrolling through job listings. His long hair falls into his eyes, his delicate features etched with frustration.

                          TED’S MOTHER (O.S.)
              Any luck, Ted?

TED turns to see his MOTHER standing in the doorway, concern etched on her face.

                          TED
              Not yet. It’s tough out there.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks into the busy employment agency, looking around nervously. He approaches the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk.

                          TED
              Hi, I’m here about the job openings.

                          RECEPTIONIST
              Take a seat, someone will be with you shortly.

INT. CLERK’S OFFICE - DAY

TED sits across from the CLERK, who flips through a stack of files.

                          CLERK
              Have you ever considered historical reenactment?

TED looks puzzled but intrigued.

                          TED
              Not really. What’s involved?

The CLERK pulls out a brochure labeled “Historical Fair” and hands it to TED.

                          CLERK
              There’s a troupe heading to a historical fair across the country. They could use someone with your look and skills.

TED examines the brochure, images of elaborate costumes and historical settings catching his eye.

                          TED
              It sounds interesting, but I’m not sure...

                          CLERK
              Give it a thought. It might be just what you’re looking for.

INT. BEDROOM - NIGHT

TED lies on his bed, staring at the brochure, deep in thought. The images of the historical fair swirl in his mind.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED stands at the reception desk, determination in his eyes.

                          TED
              I’ve decided. I’ll take the job.

The RECEPTIONIST smiles and hands him a contract.

EXT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks out of the agency, a mix of apprehension and excitement on his face as he steps into a new adventure.

                          FADE OUT.

This structure helps guide you through writing each scene with clarity and coherence. If you have specific scenes or ideas you need further assistance with, feel free to share, and I’ll be happy to help!

Polished Scenes¶

INT. BEDROOM - DAY

TED, 18, sits at his cluttered desk, staring at his computer screen, scrolling through job listings. His long hair falls into his eyes, his delicate features etched with fatigue.

                      TED’S AUNT (O.S.)
          Are you playing on your computer again, Ted?

TED turns to see his AUNT standing in the doorway, clearly frustrated.

                      TED
          I'm not playing! Looking for work is not playing.

                      TED’S AUNT
          You're never going to find anything that way, Ted.

TED rolls his eyes and turns back to his computer, focusing intently on the screen. Something has caught his eye.

                      TED
          Oh yeah?

The sound of an inkjet printer printing fills the room.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

TED walks into the busy employment agency, holding a printed sheet of paper. He approaches the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk.

                      TED
          Hi, I’m here to see Mabel Hastings.

                      RECEPTIONIST
          Please take a seat. I'll let her know you're here.

INT. CLERK’S OFFICE - DAY

TED sits across from the CLERK, who flips through a stack of files, then looks up and studies him inquisitively.

                      CLERK
          Do you have any background in historical reenactment?

TED looks taken aback.

                      TED
          Well, no, but my favorite subject in high school was history.

MABEL peers closely at him, still studying him. She seems to make up her mind and pulls out a folder labeled “Fleur de Lys,” handing a brochure to TED.

                      CLERK
          There’s a sort of historical fair I think would be an amazing opportunity for you. They are looking for someone with your looks and skills. The pay isn't great, but you get free room and board. Only downside is that it's in New South Wales...

TED examines the brochure, images of historical settings catching his eye. He pauses at the words "New South Wales."

                      TED

                      TED’S AUNT

Robyn:

Good morning, Emily. Please give the french word for the 1750 garment called 'stays' in English.

Emily:

Good morning! The French word for the 1750 garment called "stays" in English is "corset."

Robyn:

Not to be confused with what later became a far more constrictive garment, is that right?

Emily:

Yes, that's correct. In the 18th century, "corset" (or "corps" in earlier periods) referred to what we now call "stays." These stays were designed to support the torso and shape the body but were generally less constrictive and more focused on maintaining posture than the Victorian corsets of the 19th century, which became much more tightly laced and restrictive. The term "stays" was commonly used in English to describe these garments during the 18th century.

Robyn:

Thank you.

Emily:

You're welcome! If you have any more questions or need further assistance, feel free to ask.

Emily:

You're very welcome! It's fascinating how the concept of stays evolved over time. In the 18th century, they were indeed more about support and shaping, rather than the extreme constriction seen in later corsets. This detail might add an interesting layer to the historical accuracy in your screenplay. The distinction could be particularly useful for scenes involving Pauline and her role in educating Ted on the nuances of 18th-century attire.

Shotlist for "Rien que Moins"¶

Scene: TED’s Job Search and Decision¶

INT. BEDROOM - DAY

  1. WS - TED's tidy bedroom. TED sits at his desk, scrolling through job listings on his computer, classical music playing softly in the background.

  2. Establishes the setting and introduces TED's environment.

  3. MS - TED from the side, his long hair falling into his eyes, his face showing signs of fatigue.

  4. Focuses on TED's physical appearance and state of mind.

  5. CU - Computer screen showing job listings, then cutting to TED's face as he listens to music.

  6. Shows what TED is doing and listening to.

  7. OTS - AUNT standing in the doorway, looking frustrated.

  8. Introduces AUNT and her disapproval.

  9. MS - TED turning to face his AUNT.

  10. Captures their interaction.

  11. CU - TED’s eyes rolling and then focusing intently on the computer screen as something catches his attention.

  12. Shows TED’s reaction to his AUNT and his new discovery.

  13. CU - Inkjet printer printing a job listing.

  14. Indicates TED's interest in a job opportunity.

INT. EMPLOYMENT AGENCY - DAY

  1. WS - Busy employment agency with people waiting and moving around.

  2. Establishes the setting of the employment agency.

  3. MS - TED approaching the RECEPTIONIST at the front desk, holding a printed sheet of paper.

  4. Shows TED taking the next step in his job search.

  5. CU - RECEPTIONIST's face as she speaks to TED.

    • Focuses on the interaction between TED and the RECEPTIONIST.

  6. MS - TED sitting down in the waiting area, looking around.

    • Shows TED’s anticipation and nerves.

INT. MABEL’S OFFICE - DAY

  1. MS - TED sitting across from MABEL, who is flipping through a stack of files.

    • Sets the scene for their conversation.

  2. CU - MABEL’s face as she studies TED closely.

    • Shows MABEL’s scrutiny and thought process.

  3. CU - TED’s reaction, looking taken aback by MABEL’s question.

    • Captures TED’s surprise and response.

  4. OTS - MABEL handing TED a brochure labeled “Fleur de Lys.”

    • Shows the pivotal moment of TED receiving the job offer.

    • Focuses on what attracts TED to the opportunity.

  6. MS - TED looking up at MABEL, determination in his eyes.

    • Shows TED making his decision.

  7. CU - MABEL smiling and handing TED a contract, then shaking her fist victoriously.

    • Conveys MABEL’s satisfaction with TED’s decision.

INT. KITCHEN - DAY

  1. WS - TED sitting at the kitchen table, contract in front of him, AUNT pacing behind.

    • Sets the scene for their argument.

  2. MS - TED turning to face his AUNT.

    • Captures the heated exchange between them.

  3. CU - AUNT’s angry face as she scolds TED.

    • Shows the intensity of her disapproval.

  4. CU - TED’s resigned expression as he turns back to face the table.

    • Highlights TED’s frustration and determination.

  5. MS - AUNT handing TED a large beige handbag, silencing him with a look.

    • Shows the finality of AUNT’s decision.

  6. CU - TED taking the handbag, looking reluctant.

    • Captures TED’s acceptance of his AUNT’s decision.

INT. AUNT’S CAR - DAY

  1. WS - TED and his AUNT in the car, driving to the meeting point.

    • Establishes the transition to the next phase of his journey.

  2. MS - AUNT looking at TED with concern.

    • Shows the emotional tension between them.

  3. CU - TED’s face, showing a mix of apprehension and determination.

    • Highlights TED’s resolve and readiness.

  4. MS - AUNT handing TED her cardigan.

    • Conveys AUNT’s care despite her stern demeanor.

  5. CU - TED accepting the cardigan, looking both grateful and resigned.

EXT. GLADSTONE SIDEWALK - DAY

TED stands on the sidewalk, waiting. He is wearing a woman's beige cardigan and holding a large beige handbag. A large SUV pulls up, music blaring.

EMMA Hey, sunshine! You’re Ted, aren’t you?

TED nods, staring in dismay at the chattering inhabitants of the SUV: several young women in their 20s.

EMMA Well, hop in! We haven’t all day. Where's your luggage?

TED hangs his head and lifts up the handbag.

EMMA A handbag! That's all you've got? Where's all your stuff? We're not just going across town...

TED That’s the lot. All my stuff...

EMMA Right-o, c'mon, in you get, petal, get a move on!

INT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

TED climbs into the back of the SUV and plops down on the seat. He glances shyly at the girls who are wordlessly inspecting him. The door slams shut, and the SUV lurches into motion.

SARAH Um, why are you carrying a handbag?

TED My aunt gave it me for my stuff - I don't have much, you see...

LUCY And she gave you that cardigan to match?

The girls giggle. TED blushes and hangs his head.

SARAH It's okay... TED, is it? No, really, it suits you.

TED sighs. The girls’ chatter resumes, now talking about the costumes they hope to be wearing at the fair.

INT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

It is late afternoon. TED is staring out of the window. Most of the girls are asleep. LUCY has taken over driving, and EMMA is sitting next to him.

EMMA Can I ask you something, Ted?

TED Sure.

EMMA Which acting school are you in?

TED Acting school? I’m not...

EMMA Oh! So, what made you take this job, then?

TED Um, I was looking for work, so I just went to the employment agency and the clerk suggested this job.

EMMA Employment agency? Which one, the one downtown?

TED nods.

EMMA Was the clerk’s name Mabel ?

TED Yeah, I think that was her name. Why?

EMMA Oh, nothing.

Her face belies her reply, as if she suddenly realises why Ted was chosen for the job.

EXT. SUV VEHICLE - DAY

The SUV drives down a long unsealed road, then turns into a large stone archway with open wrought-iron gates. It drives up a long driveway before stopping in from of a perfect replica of a weathered 17th century French chateau. At the front door are two women, both in 1750’s attire. KATHY is dressed as a lady of nobility. PAULINE wears the dress of a maidservant.

KATHY Welcome to Fleur de Lys, mesdemoiselles!

The girls pile out of the SUV in wonderment, enchanted by the dress KATHY is wearing. TED climbs out last. He looks at the building in amazement.

KATHY Come insides, ladies, let’s get you settled in.

Pauline moves in front of TED as he moves to enter with the others, shaking her head at him. KATHY and PAULINE exchange looks, and KATHY nods.

PAULINE You are coming with me. Unless you are a ‘young lady’.

TED follows PAULINE into down the footpath to a side door. They enter.

PAULINE This is the servant’s entrance. You must know your place - this is where you will enter the chateau.

TED looks at PAULINE in puzzlement.

TED I don’t get it...

PAULINE Really? Well, you will.

INT. KITCHEN WITH STONE FIREPLACE - DAY

A hand is stirring the content’s of a large stone pot. It turns out to be Ted’s.

PAULINE (O.S.) Unless you are enrolled in an acting school and are going to be playing the role of a young lady of noble birth, this is the only position you could be given.

TED (O.S.) I was told this was a ‘historical fair’.

PAULINE (O.S.) Well, Flour de Lys is not really a fair. It’s more of a ‘historical experience’ . Our clientele come from all over the world to experience 18th century France .

TED has stopped stirring.

PAULINE Keep stirring!

TED goes back to stirring.

PAULINE Ted, must understand some very important facts. First thing you need to know is: you are my responsibility . I am responsible for your training, which is going to be extensive . You will call me ‘Miss Pauline’ . Do you understand ?

PAULINE looms over TED, who shrinks somewhat beneath her overbearing presence.

PAULINE Of course, you can pack up and go home, if you find this too hard .

TED shakes his head.

TED I signed a contract, Miss, uh, Pauline .

PAULINE Oh yes, so you did .

INT. CHATEAU DINING ROOM - EVENING

The girls are sitting around the table, dressed in beautiful 1780’s gowns. They sip wine and are chattering excitedly about their roles. PAULINE is serving soup and giving instructions to TED who is standing at the sideboard.

PAULINE You must never, ever spill anything on any of the ladies or the guests, ever !

EMMA Ted really looks out of place, doesn’t he, Miss Kathy ? We’re all wearing lovely clothing and he just looks...

KATHY Don’t worry about Ted, ladies . Pauline will make sure he’s appropriately attired tomorrow for ‘La Petite Dejeuner’ .

SARAH Ooo, I can’t wait to start learning French !

CHLOE I already speak some French - I can help you with it .

EMMA Would you be willing to help me too ?

LUCY is looking daggers at EMMA. She is in a relationship with CHLOE. KATHY smiles at them.

KATHY Don’t worry, we have lots of support available... Pauline is fluent in French, aren’t you ?

PAULINE curtseys.

PAULINE Oui, madame! Je parle couramment français .

The girls clap their hands.

INT. PAULINE”S BEDROOM - NIGHT

TED is standing nervously by the armoire as PAULINE sits on the bed. She beckons him to come to her.

PAULINE Viens ici auprès de moi, petite . Come sit here with me . We need to talk .

TED moves slowly to the bed and sits down, but not directly next to PAULINE. She moves in very close to him.

PAULINE Qu’est-que tu es mignon! You are so cute !

She is taller than he is, and full-figured. She looms over him. He seems small and frail next to her.

PAULINE It makes me happy that you are so obedient . It will make learning your role so much easier .

TED My role ?

PAULINE faces him, brings her face close to his, looks intently into his eyes.

PAULINE Yes, your role . It is going to be identical to mine . Identical .

TED Servant .

PAULINE Domestique .

TED stares at her as she hands him a garment.

PAULINE We will be up early, so we need to get some sleep. Here’s your chemise. That’s what we wear at night. Actually, that’s what we wear all the time.

TED turns it around with a puzzled face. PAULINE is undressing, stops to point to the front of the chemise.

PAULINE This is the front. We haven’t had time to get you one of your own, so you will need to wear mine for now. Vites, mon lapin, let’s get you dressed for bed.

TED Bed. Where am I sleeping?

PAULINE Here, with me. Don’t worry, I don’t bite. And I don’t snore.

PAULINE climbs into bed and pats the space next to her.

INT. PAULINE”S BEDROOM - NIGHT

TED lies in bed, wide-eyed listening to PAULINE snoring. Her arm is around his chest and her mouth is near his head.

Good morning, Emily. Please provide a fairly detailed monologue of a woman explaining to a young man how to put on the following items of 1750s female servant clothing, which he is unfamiliar with. He is already wearing the chemise.

Stays (Corset): - Structure and Purpose: Over the chemise, Sophie wears stays. These are less restrictive than those worn by higher-class women, focusing more on support than on shaping the body. - Material: The stays are made from sturdy fabric, often linen, with boning (whale baleen or reeds) to provide structure. - Lacing: They are laced up at the back or front, depending on the design, and provide a straight, supported posture.

Petticoats: - Layers and Fabric: Sophie wears one or more petticoats over her chemise and stays. These are made of wool, linen, or cotton and add warmth and modesty. - Design: The petticoats are plain and practical, without the decorative elements seen in the attire of wealthier women.

Gown (Robe): - Style and Fabric: Her gown is simple and utilitarian, made from durable fabric such as wool or coarse linen. The gown is usually in muted, practical colors like brown, grey, or dark blue, suitable for a servant’s work. - Bodice and Skirt: The bodice is fitted, often with a front-lacing or hook closure, and the skirt is full, allowing for ease of movement.

Apron: - Function and Appearance: A key component of a maid’s attire, the apron is made of sturdy linen or cotton. It is typically white or light-colored and covers the front of the gown to protect it from dirt and stains. - Style: The apron ties at the waist and may have a bib that covers the bodice, depending on the style.

Cap: - Material and Design: Sophie wears a simple, linen cap that covers her hair, keeping it neat and clean while she works. - Appearance: The cap is plain, usually white, and fits snugly around the head, with ties or a drawstring at the back or under the chin.

Footwear: - Shoes: Her shoes are practical and made from sturdy leather. They are designed for durability and comfort, with a low heel or none at all. - Stockings: Wool or cotton stockings are worn under the shoes, providing warmth and modesty.

Emily:

Certainly! Here’s a detailed monologue of a woman explaining to a young man how to put on the items of 1750s female servant clothing:

PAULINE: "Alright, Ted, listen carefully. I know all of this is new to you, but I’ll guide you through each step. You’ve already got the chemise on, so let’s move on to the stays, or as you might call them, a corset.

Now, the stays are designed to provide support and give you a straight, upright posture. They’re less restrictive than the ones worn by higher-class women, so don’t worry too much about feeling too confined. These are made from sturdy fabric, often linen, with boning made from whale baleen or reeds for structure. You’ll notice they’re laced at the back. Here, let me help you with this.

First, you need to wrap the stays around your torso. Make sure the boning is positioned to support your back and sides. See these laces? They go through these eyelets. You’ll want to pull them snug but not too tight. The goal is support, not discomfort. If you lace it up too tightly, you won’t be able to move or breathe properly. I’ll start the lacing, and you can finish it.

Next, we have the petticoats. These are worn over the chemise and stays. They’re made from wool, linen, or cotton and add warmth and modesty. You’ll be wearing more than one petticoat to give some volume and keep you warm. Here, step into this first one. Pull it up to your waist and tie it securely. Make sure it sits comfortably on your hips. Now, the second one, same as before. These are plain and practical, so don’t worry about any decorative elements – you won’t find any.

Now for the gown, or the robe. This is simple and utilitarian, made from durable fabric like wool or coarse linen. It’s usually in muted, practical colors like brown, grey, or dark blue, suitable for a servant’s work. Put your arms through the sleeves first. The bodice should fit snugly over the stays, and the skirt will be full to allow for ease of movement. There, now we’ll lace or hook the front closure. Make sure it’s secure but not too tight.

Next, we’ll add the apron. This is a key part of your attire. It’s made of sturdy linen or cotton and is typically white or light-colored. It covers the front of your gown to protect it from dirt and stains. Tie it at the waist like this. Some aprons have a bib that covers the bodice, but today we’ll use one that ties just at the waist. Make sure it’s snug but not too tight.

Now, let’s put on your cap. This simple, linen cap will cover your hair, keeping it neat and clean while you work. It’s plain white and fits snugly around your head. Here, put it on and adjust the ties under your chin or at the back, whichever feels more secure. Make sure it’s comfortable and not too tight.

Finally, we have the footwear. You’ll wear practical shoes made from sturdy leather, designed for durability and comfort. These have a low heel, or sometimes none at all. Here are your wool stockings. Put them on first – they’ll provide warmth and modesty. Now, step into the shoes. Make sure they fit well and are comfortable, as you’ll be on your feet a lot.

There you go, Ted. You’re all set. Remember, this attire is about practicality and support, not fashion. You’ll get used to it soon enough. Now, let’s get to work, shall we?"

PAULINE: "Alright, Ted, now that you’re dressed, we need to take care of your hair. Your long hair can be quite beautiful, but for practical reasons, we need to style it appropriately to fit under the maid’s cap.

First, we need to make sure your hair is clean and untangled. I’ll start by brushing it gently to remove any knots. Hold still, this might pull a bit. There we go, nice and smooth.

Next, we’ll part your hair down the middle. This helps to distribute the hair evenly on both sides, making it easier to manage. I’ll use this comb to get a straight part. Perfect.

Now, I’m going to gather your hair into two sections, one on each side of your head. I’ll braid each section loosely. These braids will help keep your hair neat and prevent it from getting tangled or messy throughout the day. Plus, they fit nicely under the cap without creating any uncomfortable lumps. Once both sides are braided, we’ll take each braid and wrap it around the crown of your head, creating a sort of braided crown. This keeps your hair off your neck and out of the way, which is very practical for your work. I’ll pin the braids in place with these hairpins. Make sure they’re secure, but not too tight. We don’t want you to get a headache.

Now that your hair is neatly braided and pinned, it’s time to put on the cap. This linen cap will cover your hair completely, keeping it neat and clean while you work. It’s plain, usually white, and fits snugly around your head. Here, let me show you how to put it on.

First, place the cap at the front of your head, just above your forehead. Make sure it’s centered. Then, pull it back over your braids, tucking in any loose strands of hair as you go. The cap should fit snugly but comfortably. Once it’s in place, tie the drawstrings at the back or under your chin, depending on what feels more secure for you. There, how does that feel?

Perfect! Now your hair is all set and you look every bit the part of a proper maid. This style will keep your hair neat and out of the way, allowing you to focus on your duties without any distractions. Let’s get started with your training, shall we?"
