Robyn:

Hi Emily. Question about footwear for women in the 1750s: was there alternative footwear to shoes?

Emily:

Hi! In the 1750s, while shoes were the primary form of footwear for women, there were a few alternative options and variations based on occasion, social status, and environment. Here are some alternatives and variations to consider:

Alternative Footwear Options:¶

  1. Mules (Slippers):
  2. Description: Mules were backless shoes that could easily be slipped on and off. They were often worn indoors as casual or house shoes.

  3. Materials: Made from leather, silk, or brocade, sometimes with decorative embroidery or embellishments.

  4. Pattens:

  5. Description: Pattens were wooden or metal overshoes worn over regular shoes to protect them from mud and wet ground. They elevated the wearer a few inches off the ground.

  6. Materials: Typically made of wood with metal fittings or entirely from metal. The design included a wooden sole with metal supports.

  7. Clogs:

  8. Description: Similar to pattens, clogs were also used to protect shoes from dirt and water. They were more commonly used by working-class women.

  9. Materials: Made from wood, sometimes with leather straps.

  10. Stockings:

  11. Description: While not footwear per se, stockings were an essential part of a woman’s attire. Made from wool, silk, or cotton, they provided warmth and modesty.
  12. Materials: Depending on the social status, they could range from coarse wool to fine silk.

Contextual Footwear Usage:¶

  • Indoors:
  • Women often wore more delicate and decorative slippers or mules when indoors. These were less about protection and more about comfort and fashion.
  • Outdoors:
  • Sturdier shoes with thicker soles were worn outside. When conditions were particularly poor (e.g., muddy or rainy), pattens or clogs were used to keep shoes clean and dry.

Footwear for Different Classes:¶

  • Upper Class:
  • Women of higher social status wore finely made shoes, often with decorative elements like buckles, bows, and embroidery. Materials included silk, brocade, and fine leather.
  • Working Class:
  • Working-class women wore more practical, durable footwear. Their shoes were made from sturdier leather and were designed for comfort and longevity. Pattens and clogs were more commonly used among this group.

Footwear in Specific Situations:¶

  • Formal Events:
  • For formal occasions and social gatherings, women wore their best shoes, often with heels and intricate designs.
  • Household Work:
  • During household chores, simpler and more comfortable shoes or slippers were preferred.

Overall, while shoes were the predominant form of footwear, alternatives like mules, pattens, and clogs provided functional variations suited to different environments and activities. If you need any more details or have specific questions, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Hi Emily. I need to refine the question to female servantsvin the 1750s: were there alternatives to shoes?

Emily:

For female servants in the 1750s, practical and durable footwear was essential due to the nature of their work. While shoes were the primary form of footwear, there were some alternatives or supplementary options that servants might have used:

Alternatives to Shoes for Female Servants in the 1750s¶

  1. Pattens:
  2. Description: Pattens were overshoes designed to protect regular shoes from mud, dirt, and wet conditions. They elevated the wearer a few inches off the ground.
  3. Usage: Commonly used outdoors or in muddy conditions to keep shoes clean and dry.

  4. Materials: Made from wood with metal fittings or entirely from metal. They featured a wooden sole with metal supports that strapped over the shoes.

  5. Clogs:

  6. Description: Similar to pattens, clogs were wooden shoes or overshoes that provided protection against wet and dirty conditions.
  7. Usage: More frequently used by working-class women and servants for outdoor tasks.

  8. Materials: Typically made from wood, sometimes with leather straps for securing the foot.

  9. Stockings and Bare Feet:

  10. Stockings: Servants often wore stockings made from wool or linen. While stockings were not footwear, they provided some level of protection and modesty.
  11. Bare Feet: In some cases, especially indoors or in warm weather, servants might work barefoot. This was more common in less formal or rural settings.

Contextual Usage:¶

  • Indoors:
  • Indoors, servants typically wore their regular shoes, often made from sturdy leather. When performing lighter tasks, they might wear simpler slippers or even go barefoot for comfort.
  • Outdoors:
  • Pattens and clogs were particularly useful for outdoor tasks where the ground was wet or muddy. These items helped preserve the life of their regular shoes by keeping them clean and dry.

Footwear Materials and Durability:¶

  • Servant Shoes:
  • Made from durable leather, these shoes were designed to withstand daily wear and tear. They were practical, without the decorative elements seen in upper-class footwear.
  • Maintenance:
  • Servants often had to maintain their footwear, cleaning and repairing them as needed to ensure longevity.

Social and Economic Considerations:¶

  • Affordability:
  • Servants' footwear was typically more affordable and less ornate than that of higher social classes. Practicality and durability were prioritized over fashion.
  • Work Environment:
  • The type of footwear worn could vary depending on the work environment and specific tasks performed. For example, a kitchen maid might have different needs compared to a housemaid working in more formal areas of the house.

In summary, while shoes were the primary form of footwear for female servants in the 1750s, alternatives like pattens and clogs were used to protect their shoes in specific conditions. Stockings provided additional coverage, and going barefoot was not uncommon in certain contexts.

Maintenance: - Cleaning: These slippers were easy to clean. Leather could be wiped down, while wool and felt could be brushed off or spot cleaned. - Durability: While not as durable as outdoor shoes, these slippers were sturdy enough to withstand regular indoor use.

Practical Usage:¶

Good evening, Emily. Please give me a detail list of morning chores, tasks and duties of the typical 1740s maidservant. I only need the tasks of the morning, but I need quite detailed information.

Emily:

Good evening! Here’s a detailed list of the typical morning chores, tasks, and duties of a maidservant in the 1740s:

Morning Chores and Duties of a 1740s Maidservant¶

1. Early Rising: - Time: Maidservants usually rose very early, often before dawn, around 5:00 AM or earlier. - Preparation: They would quickly dress in their work attire, typically a chemise, stays, petticoats, gown, apron, and cap.

2. Starting the Fires: - Task: One of the first duties was to light the household fires. This included the kitchen hearth, fireplaces in the main living areas, and sometimes the bedrooms of the household members. - Procedure: Clean out the ashes from the previous day, lay fresh kindling, and light the fire. Ensure a good supply of firewood and coal is ready for use throughout the day.

3. Fetching Water: - Task: Fetch water from the well, pump, or nearby water source. - Procedure: Use buckets to carry water to the kitchen and other necessary areas. Fill water pitchers, basins, and cisterns for household use.

4. Preparing the Kitchen: - Task: Prepare the kitchen for the day’s activities. - Procedure: Light the kitchen fire, ensure all cooking utensils are clean and ready for use, and check the pantry and larder for ingredients needed for breakfast.

5. Breakfast Preparation: - Task: Assist the cook, if there is one, in preparing breakfast for the household. - Procedure: This could include baking bread, preparing tea or coffee, cooking porridge, eggs, or other breakfast items.

6. Cleaning the Kitchen and Scullery: - Task: Clean the kitchen area after breakfast preparation. - Procedure: Wash dishes, pots, and pans. Sweep and mop the kitchen floor, wipe down surfaces, and ensure the scullery is clean and organized.

7. Setting the Breakfast Table: - Task: Set the dining room or breakfast room table. - Procedure: Lay out tablecloths, place settings, silverware, and crockery. Serve breakfast dishes and ensure everything is in place before the family arrives.

8. Tending to the Bedrooms: - Task: Tidy the bedrooms of the household members. - Procedure: Make the beds, tidy up personal items, open curtains or shutters, and ensure the rooms are aired out. Empty chamber pots and refill water pitchers.

9. Cleaning Duties: - Task: General cleaning of the house. - Procedure: Sweep and mop floors in the main living areas, dust furniture, and polish surfaces. Clean the entrance hall and stairs.

10. Laundry Duties: - Task: Begin the laundry process, if it is laundry day. - Procedure: Collect dirty linens and clothing, begin washing items by hand in large tubs, scrub with soap, and rinse. Hang laundry to dry, either indoors or outside.

11. Polishing and Maintenance: - Task: Polishing and maintaining household items. - Procedure: Polish silverware, brass, and copper items. Sharpen knives and perform minor maintenance tasks as needed.

12. Attending to Household Members: - Task: Assist household members with their morning routines. - Procedure: Help with dressing, hairdressing, and other personal tasks as needed. Deliver hot water for washing and ensure their rooms are comfortable.

13. Checking Supplies: - Task: Take stock of household supplies. - Procedure: Check the pantry, larder, and storage areas for necessary supplies. Make note of items that need to be replenished and inform the housekeeper or cook.

14. Preparing for the Day’s Main Meal: - Task: Begin preparations for the main meal of the day, usually served in the early afternoon. - Procedure: Assist in preliminary cooking tasks, such as peeling vegetables, preparing meats, and setting up cooking equipment.

These detailed tasks outline a typical morning for a maidservant in the 1740s, showcasing the breadth of responsibilities and the busy nature of their workday. If you need more specific details or additional tasks, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Hi Emily. What an adventure this is turning out to be. I've got TED fully dressed as an 18th century maid starting fires in the hearth and now, fetching water from a well in the early dawn. How did they fetch water out of a well in those days?

Emily:

Fetching water from a well in the 18th century was a common and essential task, especially for households without direct access to running water. Here’s a detailed description of how this was typically done:

Fetching Water from a Well in the 18th Century¶

1. Well Construction: - Structure: Wells were often stone or brick-lined shafts dug deep into the ground to access underground water sources. The top of the well was usually covered with a well house or a simple structure to protect the water from contamination and debris. - Covering: Many wells had a wooden cover or a small roof to prevent dirt and leaves from falling in.

2. Drawing Water: - Bucket and Rope: The most common method involved using a wooden or metal bucket attached to a rope. The rope would be either wound around a windlass (a horizontal drum turned by a handle) or simply tied to the bucket and pulled up manually. - Windlass: A windlass consisted of a rotating horizontal cylinder mounted on a frame, with a handle for turning. The rope would be wound around this cylinder. By turning the handle, the bucket could be lowered into the well and then raised once filled with water.

3. Steps to Fetch Water: - Approach the Well: Ted, dressed as an 18th-century maid, would approach the well with a clean bucket. - Remove Cover: If the well had a cover, he would remove it to access the opening. - Lower the Bucket: Ted would lower the bucket into the well by unwinding the rope from the windlass or manually lowering it until it reached the water. - Fill the Bucket: Once the bucket touched the water, it would begin to fill. Sometimes a slight tug or shake of the rope was needed to ensure the bucket tipped and filled completely. - Raise the Bucket: After the bucket was filled, Ted would turn the handle of the windlass to wind the rope back up, lifting the bucket out of the well. If there was no windlass, he would pull the rope up hand-over-hand. - Secure the Water: Upon reaching the top, Ted would secure the bucket on the edge of the well or on the ground to avoid spillage. - Pour into Larger Containers: He would then pour the water into larger containers or water barrels for household use.

4. Additional Considerations: - Repeat Process: Depending on the amount of water needed, this process might be repeated several times. - Cleanliness: It was important to keep the bucket and rope clean to ensure the water remained uncontaminated. - Physical Effort: Fetching water required significant physical effort, particularly if the well was deep or if large quantities of water were needed.

Visual Description:¶

Imagine Ted, standing at the well in the early dawn light, dressed in his maid's attire. He carefully removes the wooden cover, revealing the dark, deep well. He attaches the bucket to the rope and begins to unwind the windlass, lowering the bucket into the depths. The sound of the bucket splashing into the water echoes up the well shaft. He then winds the handle, slowly lifting the heavy, water-filled bucket back to the surface. With a practiced hand, he secures the bucket and pours the precious water into a larger container, ready to carry it back to the chateau.

This task, while labor-intensive, was a crucial part of daily life in the 18th century, ensuring the household had the necessary water for cooking, cleaning, and other essential activities.

Robyn:

Good morning, Emily. In the film 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu" (Portrait of a Young Lady on Fire), Heloise has obtained a substance that the women apply to their armpits. Without going into great detail, what was this substance? And was there actually such a substance back in the 18th century?

Emily:

Good morning, Robyn! In the film "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu" (Portrait of a Lady on Fire), the substance that Héloïse and Marianne apply to their armpits is a concoction made from lemon juice and herbs. This mixture is used as a natural deodorant.

Historical Context:¶

In the 18th century, personal hygiene and grooming practices were quite different from today. While commercial deodorants as we know them did not exist, people did use natural substances to manage body odor.

Common Substances Used:¶

  1. Lemon Juice:
  2. Properties: Lemon juice was known for its antibacterial properties and fresh scent.

  3. Usage: It was often used as a natural deodorant to help reduce body odor. The acidity of lemon juice can help kill bacteria that cause odor.

  4. Herbs:

  5. Properties: Herbs such as rosemary, lavender, and sage were used for their pleasant scents and potential antibacterial effects.
  6. Usage: These herbs could be crushed and applied directly, or infused into water or oil for use as a rinse or rub.

Application:¶

People in the 18th century, especially those in higher social classes, might have used a mixture of lemon juice and herbs as a form of deodorant. This would be applied to the skin, including the armpits, to help mask body odor and provide a sense of cleanliness.

Historical Accuracy:¶

While the specific mixture shown in the film may not have been documented in historical records, the use of lemon juice and herbs as natural deodorants is consistent with known 18th-century hygiene practices.

So, in summary, the substance in the film is a mixture of lemon juice and herbs, used as a natural deodorant. This practice is indeed reflective of the types of natural remedies that would have been used during that time period.

Robyn:

Further to this, Heloise ended up with dilated pupils and a sense of euphoria, which suggests that the substance included some herb or compound that would render that effect. She claimed that it would "make time seem longer". Does anything suggest itself that would result in dilated pupils and a sense of timelessness, from that period?

Emily:

Yes, there is a substance that fits this description and was known in historical contexts: belladonna (also known as deadly nightshade). Belladonna is a plant that contains tropane alkaloids, such as atropine, which can cause pupil dilation and a sense of euphoria. Here are some details:

Belladonna (Deadly Nightshade)¶

Scientific Name: Atropa belladonna

Historical Use: - Cosmetics: In the Renaissance and later periods, women used belladonna to dilate their pupils, which was considered a beauty enhancement. The name "belladonna" means "beautiful lady" in Italian. - Medicinal: It was used in small doses to treat various ailments, including muscle pain, inflammation, and as a sedative.

Effects: - Dilated Pupils: Atropine, a compound in belladonna, blocks the action of acetylcholine in the muscles of the eye, leading to pupil dilation. - Euphoria and Hallucinations: In larger doses, belladonna can cause euphoria, hallucinations, and a sense of altered time perception. - Dangerous: It is important to note that belladonna is highly toxic, and improper use can lead to severe poisoning or death.

Historical Context:¶

In the 18th century, while not commonly documented for recreational use, belladonna and similar plants were known for their potent effects and were sometimes used in folk remedies and practices. The symptoms described in the film—dilated pupils and a sense of euphoria—align well with the effects of belladonna.

Application in the Film Context:¶

  • Topical Application: Belladonna could be infused in an oil or mixed with other substances and applied topically. This might not be the typical method for inducing strong effects, but it could still lead to mild absorption through the skin.
  • Herbal Mixture: It could have been part of a more complex herbal mixture applied in small amounts to achieve the desired effect without severe toxicity.

Conclusion:¶

In "Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu," the substance likely included a small amount of belladonna or a similar tropane alkaloid-containing plant, which would cause dilated pupils and a sense of euphoria, making time seem longer. This fits the historical use of such plants and their documented effects.

If you need more detailed historical references or further explanation, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Is there an association between belladona and the medication we know today called 'Atropine', which generally has cardio-active properties?

Emily:

Yes, there is a direct association between belladonna and atropine. Atropine is one of the primary active compounds found in the belladonna plant (Atropa belladonna) and other plants in the Solanaceae family, such as henbane (Hyoscyamus niger) and jimsonweed (Datura stramonium).

Relationship Between Belladonna and Atropine:¶

  1. Source:
  2. Belladonna: Atropa belladonna, commonly known as deadly nightshade, contains several tropane alkaloids, including atropine, hyoscyamine, and scopolamine.

  3. Atropine: This is a specific alkaloid derived from belladonna and other related plants.

  4. Historical Use:

  5. In historical contexts, extracts from belladonna plants were used for their medicinal properties, such as muscle relaxation, pain relief, and dilating pupils.

  6. The cosmetic use of belladonna for pupil dilation led to its name, "beautiful lady" in Italian.

  7. Modern Medicine:

  8. Atropine: Today, atropine is a well-known medication used in various medical settings. It is used to:
    • Treat bradycardia (slow heart rate) by increasing the heart rate.
    • Dilate pupils during eye examinations.
    • Reduce saliva production during surgery.
    • Act as an antidote for certain types of poisoning, including organophosphates and nerve agents.

  9. Atropine works by blocking the action of the neurotransmitter acetylcholine at muscarinic receptors, which affects the parasympathetic nervous system.

  10. Effects:

  11. Pupil Dilation: One of the notable effects of atropine is mydriasis (pupil dilation), which is why belladonna was historically used in cosmetics.
  12. Euphoria and Hallucinations: In higher doses, atropine can cross the blood-brain barrier and cause central nervous system effects, including euphoria, hallucinations, and a sense of altered time perception.

Summary:¶

Atropine is a purified and concentrated form of one of the active compounds found in belladonna. The historical use of belladonna for its medicinal and cosmetic properties directly relates to the effects of atropine, which is used in modern medicine for its various pharmacological actions, particularly on the heart and eyes.

So, to answer your question, yes, there is a strong association between belladonna and atropine. The compound atropine is derived from belladonna and shares many of the same properties that made belladonna notable in historical practices.

Robyn:

Thank you for providing so much crucial historial information about the 18th century, Emily. In order to store and transport substances that needed preserving, what sorts of containers did 18th century women have access to?

Emily:

In the 18th century, women had access to a variety of containers for storing and transporting substances that needed preserving. These containers were made from materials that could keep contents fresh and safe from contamination. Here are some common types of containers used during that period:

1. Glass Bottles and Jars¶

  • Material: Glass
  • Usage: Used for storing liquids such as oils, vinegar, wines, spirits, and medicinal concoctions. Also used for preserves like jams and pickles.
  • Sealing: Stoppers made from cork or glass, sometimes sealed with wax to prevent air entry.

2. Ceramic and Earthenware Jars¶

  • Material: Glazed or unglazed ceramic and earthenware
  • Usage: Ideal for storing dry goods such as grains, flour, spices, and dried herbs. Also used for fermented foods like sauerkraut.
  • Sealing: Cloth covers tied with string or leather, and sometimes wooden lids.

3. Stoneware¶

  • Material: Stoneware, a type of pottery fired at high temperatures
  • Usage: Sturdy and impermeable, used for storing liquids, pickles, and preserved foods.
  • Sealing: Often had tight-fitting lids or were sealed with cloth and wax.

4. Wooden Barrels and Casks¶

  • Material: Wood, typically oak
  • Usage: Used for bulk storage and transportation of liquids such as beer, wine, cider, and sometimes dry goods.
  • Sealing: Tight-fitting wooden lids, sealed with pitch or tar to prevent leakage.

5. Metal Containers¶

  • Material: Tin, pewter, copper
  • Usage: Tin-lined copper pots were used for cooking and storing perishable items. Pewter flasks and tankards for liquids.
  • Sealing: Metal lids or stoppers, sometimes combined with cork or leather for a tighter seal.

6. Leather Bottles and Flasks¶

  • Material: Treated leather
  • Usage: Portable containers for liquids, often used by travelers.
  • Sealing: Cork stoppers or tied-off openings.

7. Wicker Baskets¶

  • Material: Wicker or woven reeds
  • Usage: Used for transporting fresh produce, eggs, and other goods that did not require airtight storage.
  • Sealing: Often lined with cloth to protect contents.

8. Cloth Sacks and Bags¶

  • Material: Linen, cotton, burlap
  • Usage: Used for storing and transporting dry goods like grains, flour, and dried fruits.
  • Sealing: Tied with string or sewn shut.

9. Waxed Paper and Parchment¶

  • Material: Parchment or cloth coated with beeswax
  • Usage: Used to wrap foods like cheese, butter, and baked goods to keep them fresh.
  • Sealing: Tied with string or sealed with additional wax.

10. Horn and Shell Containers¶

  • Material: Hollowed-out animal horns or large seashells
  • Usage: Used for storing small quantities of dry goods, spices, or snuff.
  • Sealing: Often fitted with wooden or cork stoppers.

Practical Considerations:¶

  • Durability: Containers needed to be durable to withstand daily use and transportation.
  • Preservation: Materials and sealing methods were chosen to maximize preservation, keeping contents fresh and uncontaminated.
  • Portability: Some containers, like leather flasks and wicker baskets, were designed for easy transportation.

These containers were crucial for everyday life in the 18th century, enabling women to store and preserve food, medicine, and other substances effectively. If you need more specific details or further assistance, feel free to ask!

Emily, I am using Highland (the software for Mac) for writing my screenplay. I have not yet been able to determine how best to enter footnotes for myself of as guidance to those reading the screenplay. I am using '[' and ']' to enter notes. Example:

Emily:

Highland is a great tool for writing screenplays, and using notes can be very helpful for both yourself and others who might read the screenplay. In Highland, you can use inline notes or comments to add footnotes or guidance without affecting the screenplay's formatting. Here's how you can do it effectively:

Using Brackets for Inline Notes¶

Your current method using brackets is straightforward and effective for adding inline notes. It keeps your notes visible in the text, which is useful for personal reminders or guidance for readers.

Example:¶

INT. THERAPY ROOM - DAY

[Ted doesn’t enjoy the solidarity experienced by Sophia in ‘Portrait’ during her procedure. Ted experiences 'The Therapy' alone: comfort only comes afterwards, and in a maternal fashion through Pauline. This is a deliberate distinction, as the message so far is designed to be quite different from ‘Portrait’.]

TED lies on the therapy table, staring at the ceiling, clearly uncomfortable.

Using Highland's Built-in Comments¶

Highland also supports comments that you can use to leave notes. Comments in Highland are not rendered in the final screenplay, making them perfect for personal notes or guidance for readers. You can enter comments by using // at the beginning of a line.

Example:¶

INT. THERAPY ROOM - DAY

// Note: Ted doesn’t enjoy the solidarity experienced by Sophia in ‘Portrait’ during her procedure. Ted experiences 'The Therapy' alone: comfort only comes afterwards, and in a maternal fashion through Pauline. This is a deliberate distinction, as the message so far is designed to be quite different from ‘Portrait’.

TED lies on the therapy table, staring at the ceiling, clearly uncomfortable.

Using Both Methods¶

You can mix both methods to separate visible notes from hidden comments.

Example:¶

INT. THERAPY ROOM - DAY

// This note is for personal guidance and won't appear in the final document
// Note: Ted doesn’t enjoy the solidarity experienced by Sophia in ‘Portrait’ during her procedure. Ted experiences 'The Therapy' alone: comfort only comes afterwards, and in a maternal fashion through Pauline. This is a deliberate distinction, as the message so far is designed to be quite different from ‘Portrait’.

[Ted doesn’t enjoy the solidarity experienced by Sophia in ‘Portrait’ during her procedure. Ted experiences 'The Therapy' alone: comfort only comes afterwards, and in a maternal fashion through Pauline. This is a deliberate distinction, as the message so far is designed to be quite different from ‘Portrait’.]

TED lies on the therapy table, staring at the ceiling, clearly uncomfortable.

Using these methods, you can effectively manage your notes and comments in Highland while keeping your screenplay organized and readable. If you need further assistance with Highland or anything else, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Further to the screenplay writing in Highland, is there an acceptable way to communicate the use of music or sound effects?

Emily:

In screenwriting, it’s important to clearly indicate music and sound effects (SFX) to guide the director, sound designer, and other members of the production team. In Highland, as in other screenwriting software, you can use specific formatting to make these elements stand out. Here’s how you can effectively communicate the use of music or sound effects in your screenplay:

Indicating Music¶

When you want to specify music, you can use a combination of uppercase and parenthetical notes to make it clear. Here’s a general guideline:

  1. Song Titles: Write the song title in uppercase.
  2. Description: Include a brief description if needed.
  3. Parentheticals: Use parentheticals to indicate specific instructions about the music.

Example:¶

INT. THERAPY ROOM - DAY

TED lies on the therapy table, staring at the ceiling, clearly uncomfortable.

(MUSIC: "Moonlight Sonata" by Beethoven starts playing softly in the background.)

TED closes his eyes, trying to relax.

Indicating Sound Effects (SFX)¶

For sound effects, you should also use uppercase to make them stand out in the text. Be clear and specific about the type of sound and its source if necessary.

Example:¶

INT. OLD HOUSE - NIGHT

The house is eerily silent. TED steps cautiously across the creaky floorboards.

SFX: CREAKING FLOORBOARDS

A sudden BANG startles him, coming from the room upstairs.

SFX: LOUD BANG FROM UPSTAIRS

Combining Music and SFX¶

You might need to combine both music and sound effects in a scene. Ensure each element is clearly indicated.

Example:¶

INT. LIVING ROOM - NIGHT

TED sits alone in the dimly lit room. The sound of a record player starting up is heard.

SFX: RECORD PLAYER STARTING

(MUSIC: "Clair de Lune" by Debussy begins to play, filling the room with a haunting melody.)

TED stands up, walking towards the window.

SFX: WIND HOWLING OUTSIDE

Using Comments for Further Instructions¶

You can also use comments to provide additional instructions or context for the music and sound effects.

Example:¶

INT. THERAPY ROOM - DAY

// Note: Use "Moonlight Sonata" by Beethoven to create a calming atmosphere.
// The music should start softly and build as the scene progresses.

(MUSIC: "Moonlight Sonata" by Beethoven starts playing softly in the background.)

TED lies on the therapy table, staring at the ceiling, clearly uncomfortable.

TED closes his eyes, trying to relax.

Using Action Lines for Context¶

You can also use action lines to provide context or describe the intended impact of the music or sound effects.

Example:¶

INT. DARK FOREST - NIGHT

The wind howls through the trees, creating an unsettling atmosphere.

SFX: WIND HOWLING

In the distance, the faint sound of a piano playing can be heard.

(MUSIC: "Nocturne in E-flat Major" by Chopin, played faintly.)

TED walks cautiously through the forest, the music growing louder as he approaches an old, abandoned cabin.

By following these guidelines, you can effectively communicate the use of music and sound effects in your screenplay. This helps ensure your creative vision is clearly understood by everyone involved in the production. If you have any more questions or need further assistance, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

I read the screenplay for 'Portrait de la Jeune Fille en Feu' - the English translation, that is (wish I could find it in the original French) - by Celine Sciamma. In the screenplay there are fairly frequent references to 'BEAT'. What is that? What is it meant to accomplish?

Emily:

In screenwriting, the term "BEAT" is a common shorthand used to indicate a pause or a moment of silence. It serves several purposes:

Purposes of "BEAT" in Screenwriting¶

  1. Pause for Impact:
  2. Dramatic Effect: A beat allows for a moment of reflection, tension, or emphasis on what has just been said or done. It gives the audience a moment to absorb the information.

  3. Emotional Resonance: It can heighten the emotional impact of a scene by providing a pause for the characters and audience to process the emotion.

  4. Timing and Rhythm:

  5. Pacing: Beats help control the pacing of dialogue and action, ensuring that scenes flow naturally and effectively.

  6. Natural Speech: In dialogue, a beat mimics natural speech patterns, indicating where a character might pause for breath or to think.

  7. Non-Verbal Communication:

  8. Subtext: A beat can indicate a character’s internal thought process or reaction without them speaking. It shows hesitation, realization, or internal conflict.
  9. Body Language: It often accompanies a physical action or a significant look, adding depth to the non-verbal communication between characters.

How "BEAT" is Used in Screenplays¶

A beat is typically placed in the action or parenthetical section of the screenplay. Here are a few examples of how "BEAT" can be used effectively:

Example 1: Dialogue Pause¶

SARAH
I can't believe you did that.

BEAT

TED
Neither can I.

Example 2: Emotional Impact¶

EMMA
I'm leaving tomorrow.

BEAT

She watches for his reaction. He looks away, unable to meet her eyes.

Example 3: Non-Verbal Reaction¶

INT. LIVING ROOM - NIGHT

Ted sits alone, staring at the letter in his hands.

BEAT

He crumples it up and throws it in the fire.

Example 4: Physical Action¶

LUCY
You didn't tell me the whole truth, did you?

BEAT

Ted takes a deep breath, looking down at his hands.

Conclusion¶

In summary, "BEAT" is a versatile tool in screenwriting that helps to control the rhythm, pacing, and emotional impact of a scene. It indicates a pause that can be used for various dramatic purposes, making it an essential part of effective storytelling.

If you have any more questions about screenwriting techniques or need further assistance, feel free to ask!

Emily:

Absolutely! "BEAT" is a subtle but powerful tool that can add depth and nuance to your screenplay. It helps convey emotions, control pacing, and emphasize key moments without needing additional dialogue. If you have more questions or need further assistance with your writing, just let me know. Happy writing!

Robyn:

I need to describe an action. It's after having achieved a coup-de-grace, the person does this thing with her fist, often accompanied by a "Yes!" Can you describe that act?

Emily:

Certainly! The action you're describing is commonly known as a "fist pump." It's a celebratory gesture where a person clenches their fist and thrusts it into the air, often accompanied by an exclamation like "Yes!" to express triumph, excitement, or victory.

Description:¶

INT. ARENA - DAY

The crowd erupts in cheers as SHEILA lands the final, decisive blow. The opponent falls, and the referee signals the end of the match.

SHEILA, breathing heavily, takes a moment to realize her victory. A wide grin spreads across her face. She clenches her fist and thrusts it into the air in a triumphant fist pump.

SHEILA Yes!

The crowd’s roar intensifies, matching the exhilaration of the moment.

Detailed Description:¶

Fist Pump: - Formation: She clenches her fingers tightly into her palm, forming a solid fist. - Motion: With a burst of energy, she extends her arm upward in a swift, powerful motion, her fist leading the way. - Expression: Her face lights up with a wide grin, eyes sparkling with triumph. - Accompaniment: She shouts "Yes!" in a voice full of exhilaration, her declaration of victory echoing through the arena.

This gesture effectively conveys her overwhelming sense of accomplishment and excitement, capturing the essence of the victorious moment.

Robyn:

Is it still a fist pump if the fist is drawn down to the side as opposed to punching the air with it?

Emily:

Yes, the gesture you're describing is often referred to as a "fist pump" as well, but more specifically, it can be called a "downward fist pump." This gesture involves drawing the clenched fist down towards the side, usually accompanied by a "Yes!" or similar exclamation. It's still a celebratory gesture that conveys excitement and triumph.

Description:¶

INT. ARENA - DAY

The crowd erupts in cheers as SHEILA lands the final, decisive blow. The opponent falls, and the referee signals the end of the match.

SHEILA, breathing heavily, takes a moment to realize her victory. A wide grin spreads across her face. She clenches her fist and draws it down sharply to her side in a triumphant downward fist pump.

SHEILA Yes!

The crowd’s roar intensifies, matching the exhilaration of the moment.

Detailed Description:¶

Downward Fist Pump: - Formation: She clenches her fingers tightly into her palm, forming a solid fist. - Motion: With a burst of energy, she draws her arm downward towards her side in a swift, powerful motion, her fist leading the way. - Expression: Her face lights up with a wide grin, eyes sparkling with triumph. - Accompaniment: She shouts "Yes!" in a voice full of exhilaration, her declaration of victory echoing through the arena.

This gesture effectively conveys her overwhelming sense of accomplishment and excitement, capturing the essence of the victorious moment.

Robyn:

Thank you - that I shall do. Also, when I read Celine Sciamma's screenplay, something that sort-of jumped out at me was punctuation. There was oftentimes a space between the last letter of a word in a sentence and, say, a question mark. Is that a screenwriting convention or just a foible of hers?

Emily:

The practice of adding a space before punctuation, such as a question mark or exclamation point, is not a standard screenwriting convention. It is more likely a stylistic choice or foible unique to Céline Sciamma or the translation you read. Screenwriting conventions generally adhere to standard punctuation rules, where no extra space is added before punctuation marks.

Standard Screenwriting Punctuation Rules¶

  • Periods and Commas: No space before; one space after.
  • Example: "I'm not sure what to do next."
  • Question Marks and Exclamation Points: No space before; one space after.
  • Example: "Are you coming with us?"
  • Example: "Watch out!"

Céline Sciamma's Style¶

Céline Sciamma might have chosen this spacing for aesthetic or readability reasons, or it could be a quirk of the formatting in the specific version of the screenplay you read. Some writers have unique typographical preferences that set their work apart, but these are not typically adopted as industry standards.

When Following Conventions¶

For your own screenplay, it’s best to follow the standard punctuation rules to ensure clarity and professionalism. Deviating from these norms can be distracting to readers and may be viewed as a mistake rather than a stylistic choice.

Example of Standard Formatting¶

INT. LIVING ROOM - DAY

TED
Are you sure this is the right way?

PAULINE
Yes, absolutely!

In Summary¶

The spacing before punctuation you noticed in Sciamma’s screenplay is likely a personal or formatting choice rather than a screenwriting convention. Stick to standard punctuation rules unless you have a specific reason for adopting a different style, and always aim for clarity and professionalism in your writing. If you have any more questions or need further assistance, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

On the voices note, I already have Pauline peppering her dialogue with little French idioms, like terms of dendearment. How does one punctuate foreign language speech, if at all?

Emily:

In screenwriting, it's important to ensure that any foreign language speech is clear and easily understood by the reader, even if they don't speak the language. Here are some general guidelines on how to punctuate and format foreign language speech:

Guidelines for Punctuating Foreign Language Speech¶

  1. Italics: Use italics for foreign words or phrases to distinguish them from the rest of the text.
  2. Translation: Provide an English translation if the meaning is not clear from the context. This can be done in a parenthetical or immediately following the foreign phrase.
  3. Consistency: Be consistent in your formatting throughout the screenplay.

Example with Pauline¶

Dialogue with French Terms of Endearment:

INT. KITCHEN - DAY

PAULINE
(victory note in her voice)
Vous avez bien fait, mon cher. You did well, my dear.

TED
Thank you, Pauline.

PAULINE
Now, let's move on to the next task. Allez, vite! Come, quickly!

Detailed Example¶

Scene with Mixed Language Dialogue:

INT. SERVANTS' QUARTERS - DAY

PAULINE
(gesturing to the table)
Apportez-moi les légumes, s'il vous plaît. Bring me the vegetables, please.

TED
Here they are.

PAULINE
Merci. Thank you.

TED
What do I do next?

PAULINE
(smiling warmly)
Chéri, you need to chop them finely. Like this.

TED
Got it.

PAULINE
You are learning quickly, mon petit. My little one.

Notes on Usage¶

  1. Italics for Foreign Words/Phrases: Italics help distinguish the foreign language from the rest of the text and make it clear to the reader that these are non-English words.
  2. Parenthetical Translations: Use parentheticals for brief translations or to explain terms of endearment if the context doesn’t make the meaning clear.
  3. Contextual Clarity: Ensure that the meaning of foreign phrases is clear from the context, especially if you choose not to provide a direct translation every time.

Additional Tips¶

  • Common Phrases: If a foreign phrase is used frequently and its meaning becomes clear through repetition, you might not need to translate it every time.
  • Consistency: Maintain consistent formatting for all foreign language speech throughout your screenplay.
  • Readability: Ensure that foreign phrases do not overwhelm the reader or distract from the flow of the story.

By following these guidelines, you can effectively incorporate foreign language speech into your screenplay, adding authenticity to Pauline's character without confusing the reader. If you need more specific examples or further assistance, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

I am struggling a bit, trying to avoid exposition. And yet, converting the concepts to dialogue seems a bit contrived. Here is the scene: TED is walking down the garden path, and EMMA intercepts him. They have this exchange:

EMMA Hey, sugar.

TED Oh, hi, er, Emma? Or did they change your name, too?

...and then, I get "lost in the weeds" of exposition:

EMMA smiles at TED and loops her arm in his, then grabs his hand and puts it on her arm, effectively changing roles. TED passively lets her do what she wants, not understanding the significance of what just occurred.

You see, at first she puts herself on his arm, but it doesn't feel right to her, so she puts him on her arm, like she's the lead (symbolic of how their relationship will unfold). How do I manage this? Do I try to convert that paragraph to dialogue somehow or leave it as is?

Emily:

Balancing exposition and dialogue can be challenging, especially when you want to convey symbolic actions and character dynamics subtly. Here’s a way to rewrite the scene to avoid heavy exposition while still conveying the significance of the gesture through action and dialogue:

Revised Scene¶

INT. GARDEN PATH - DAY

TED walks down the garden path, looking around curiously. EMMA intercepts him with a bright smile.

EMMA Hey, sugar.

TED Oh, hi, er, Emma? Or did they change your name, too?

EMMA (smiling) Nope, still Emma.

She loops her arm through his, but then pauses, sensing something off. Gently, she adjusts, taking his hand and placing it on her arm, leading him.

EMMA This feels better, don’t you think?

TED (unsure) I guess...

They walk together, Emma confidently guiding Ted, who follows her lead, still trying to understand the shift.

Explanation¶

  1. Dialogue: The initial exchange sets the tone and establishes familiarity between Ted and Emma.
  2. Action: Instead of explaining the significance in a block of exposition, show it through Emma's actions. She first tries to loop her arm through Ted's, then adjusts by taking his hand and placing it on her arm.
  3. Subtlety: Emma's line, "This feels better, don’t you think?" hints at the symbolic shift without explicitly stating it. Ted's unsure response conveys his passive acceptance and confusion.
  4. Body Language: The physical adjustment and Emma's confident leading role are shown through their actions and minimal dialogue, allowing the audience to interpret the underlying meaning.

This approach uses a combination of concise dialogue and purposeful action to convey the symbolic shift in their relationship, avoiding heavy exposition and making the scene feel more natural.

Yes, it's perfectly acceptable to use capitalized names for characters in action or description passages in screenplays. This helps to clearly identify who is present and who is performing actions, making the script easier to read and understand for everyone involved in the production.

Example 1:¶

Your first example is formatted correctly:

KATHY and PAULINE are standing at the door. From the inside of the building comes soft sobbing. The door opens, and a woman in 1740s peasant dress appears. She is the HEALER who has just administered ‘The Therapy’.

Key Points:¶

  1. Capitalized Names: Use capitalized names when characters are first introduced and whenever they perform actions. This helps readers immediately identify the characters involved.
  2. Clear Actions: Describe actions clearly and succinctly to maintain readability and flow.
  3. Introduction of New Characters: When introducing a new character, such as the HEALER, capitalizing the name helps to highlight the introduction and ensure it’s noted.

Consistency:¶

Maintaining consistency in formatting and capitalizing names helps in creating a professional and easily readable screenplay. It ensures that actors, directors, and other crew members can quickly understand who is involved in each scene and what actions are taking place.

I am assuming there is historical precedent for the lower class teaching higher class individuals how to write music or paint.

Emily:

Yes, there is historical precedent for lower-class individuals teaching higher-class individuals how to write music, paint, and engage in other artistic pursuits. This often occurred in the context of private tutors or skilled servants who were employed to educate members of the upper class. Here are a few points to support this aspect of your story:

Historical Precedent for Lower-Class Tutors¶

  1. Private Tutors:
  2. Role: Skilled individuals from lower social classes often served as private tutors for the children of wealthy families. These tutors provided instruction in various subjects, including music, painting, languages, and literature.

  3. Status: While tutors were knowledgeable and respected for their expertise, they were still considered part of the servant class and held a lower social status than the families they served.

  4. Music Masters and Maestros:

  5. Role: Many renowned composers and musicians, such as Johann Sebastian Bach and Antonio Vivaldi, taught music to the nobility and upper class. They provided private lessons, composed music for their patrons, and sometimes even lived in their patrons' homes.

  6. Compensation: These music masters were compensated for their services but were still dependent on the patronage of the wealthy for their livelihood.

  7. Art Instructors:

  8. Role: Similarly, painters and artists from lower social classes often taught the children of the nobility how to paint and draw. These instructors were valued for their skills but did not share the same social status as their students.
  9. Examples: Many famous artists, such as Peter Paul Rubens and Diego Velázquez, worked closely with noble families, providing art instruction and creating commissioned works.

Application to Your Story¶

In the context of your screenplay, TED (now Dora) becoming Emma's personal maid-servant with the primary purpose of teaching her how to write music fits well within this historical precedent. Here’s how you can integrate this aspect into your narrative:

  • Setting the Scene:
  • Emma’s Interest: Emma could have a genuine interest in learning how to write music, and Kathy sees Dora’s skills as an opportunity to elevate Emma’s education.

  • Dora’s Role: As Dora transitions into her role as Emma’s personal maid-servant, her musical abilities become a central part of her duties. This dual role emphasizes the complexity of Dora's position within the household.

  • Dialogue and Interactions:

  • Emma’s Curiosity: Emma could express curiosity and admiration for Dora’s musical talent, leading to scenes where Dora teaches her the basics of music composition.
  • Power Dynamics: The interactions between Dora and Emma can highlight the power dynamics at play, with Dora holding expertise that Emma values, yet still being subservient in social status.

Example Scene:¶

INT. MUSIC ROOM - DAY

DORA sits at the piano, playing a soft melody. EMMA watches intently, her eyes filled with curiosity.

EMMA
Can you show me how to do that?

DORA
Of course, Miss Emma. Let’s start with the basics.

DORA gently guides EMMA's hands to the piano keys, demonstrating the proper technique. EMMA follows along, her fingers stumbling at first but gradually finding the right notes.

EMMA
(smiling)
You make it look so easy.

DORA
(smiling back)
It just takes practice. You’ll get used to it.

As EMMA continues to practice, DORA watches with a mix of pride and humility, aware of the delicate balance between her role as a servant and her position as a teacher.

By incorporating these elements, you can create rich, historically accurate interactions that deepen the relationship between Dora and Emma while highlighting the complexities of their social roles. If you need further assistance or more examples, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Hi Emily. Please tell me the names of more popular modes of horse-drawn transport carriages of the 1740s, please.

Emily:

In the 1740s, various types of horse-drawn carriages were used for transportation, each serving different purposes and catering to different social classes. Here are some of the popular modes of horse-drawn transport from that period:

Popular Horse-Drawn Carriages of the 1740s¶

  1. Berline:
  2. Description: A luxurious, four-wheeled carriage with a suspended, enclosed body. Often used by nobility and the wealthy.

  3. Features: Typically had a high roof, windows with curtains, and comfortable seating. Some were elaborately decorated.

  4. Cabriolet:

  5. Description: A light, two-wheeled carriage with a folding hood, pulled by a single horse. Often used for short trips within cities or towns.

  6. Features: Open design, offering little protection from the elements but providing a quick and nimble means of transport.

  7. Chaise:

  8. Description: A light, two-wheeled carriage for one or two passengers, similar to a cabriolet but usually more enclosed.

  9. Features: Often had a fixed or collapsible top and was drawn by one horse.

  10. Coach:

  11. Description: A larger, four-wheeled carriage used for long-distance travel or by wealthy families.

  12. Features: Enclosed with doors and windows, often with a roof rack for luggage. Pulled by multiple horses (usually two or four).

  13. Curricle:

  14. Description: A two-wheeled, open carriage designed to be drawn by two horses abreast.

  15. Features: Popular among the upper classes for leisure rides, known for its speed and stylish appearance.

  16. Gig:

  17. Description: A light, two-wheeled carriage, typically used for local travel and errands.

  18. Features: Pulled by a single horse, open, and simple in design.

  19. Landau:

  20. Description: A four-wheeled carriage with a folding top divided into two sections.

  21. Features: Could be opened or closed depending on the weather, and was often used for formal occasions and ceremonies.

  22. Phaeton:

  23. Description: A sporty, open carriage with four wheels, designed for fast travel.

  24. Features: Known for its high seating position and often used by young aristocrats for leisure and showing off.

  25. Post Chaise:

  26. Description: A fast, four-wheeled carriage used for traveling long distances, often hired from post houses.

  27. Features: Enclosed with seating for two or more passengers, drawn by a team of horses.

  28. Wagon:

    • Description: A sturdy, four-wheeled vehicle used for transporting goods and passengers over rough roads.
    • Features: Less refined and more functional, drawn by multiple horses or oxen.

Visual Descriptions¶

  • Berline: Imagine an ornate, enclosed carriage with high wheels, drawn by four horses, its windows curtained and the exterior decorated with gilded trim.
  • Cabriolet: Picture a light, open carriage with a leather hood that can be raised or lowered, drawn by a single horse, perfect for quick trips through town.
  • Chaise: Visualize a small, enclosed two-wheeler, drawn by one horse, its compact body just large enough for one or two passengers.
  • Coach: Envision a grand, four-wheeled carriage with doors and windows, drawn by a team of four horses, used for long journeys across the countryside.
  • Curricle: Think of a stylish, open carriage with two large wheels, drawn by a pair of horses, designed for speed and elegance.
  • Gig: Picture a simple, open two-wheeler, pulled by a single horse, practical for everyday use.
  • Landau: Imagine a four-wheeled carriage with a split top that can be folded down, drawn by two or four horses, used for both formal and informal outings.
  • Phaeton: Visualize a high-seated, sporty carriage, open to the air, drawn by a pair of horses, used by the fashionable elite for leisurely drives.
  • Post Chaise: Envision a sturdy, enclosed carriage, drawn by a team of horses, used for long-distance travel, often seen speeding from post house to post house.
  • Wagon: Think of a large, utilitarian vehicle, designed to carry heavy loads, drawn by multiple horses or oxen, making its way along rough country roads.

These carriages each had their own unique design and purpose, reflecting the social and economic context of the 1740s. If you need further details or specific examples for your story, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Good morning, Emily. I am exploring the concept of what some of the challenges were to women in the 1750s in terms of their clothing and getting work done, particularly the servants. Please provide as much detail as possible about getting dressed on your own as a servant of the 1750s, i.e., doing up stays and putting on shoes, but also how wearing a full skirt with petticoats impacted carrying things and doing their normal chores. This question may prompt subsequent questions.

Emily:

Good morning, Robyn! The clothing of the 1750s, particularly for women and female servants, presented several challenges in terms of practicality and mobility. Here's a detailed look at the process of getting dressed and how their attire impacted their ability to perform everyday tasks:

Getting Dressed as a Female Servant in the 1750s¶

1. Chemise (Shift) - First Layer: The chemise, a loose, long-sleeved undergarment made of linen, was the first layer worn directly against the skin. It absorbed sweat and protected outer garments from body oils and dirt. - Putting It On: The chemise was simple to put on, as it slipped over the head and was often gathered at the neck with a drawstring.

2. Stays (Corset) - Structure and Support: Stays were worn over the chemise to provide structure and support. They were less restrictive for servants, focusing more on posture than on shaping the body. - Lacing: Stays were laced up the back or front. For a servant dressing alone, back-laced stays posed a significant challenge. Servants often relied on a buddy system, where they helped each other lace up. In some cases, stays might be partially laced up the back and finished at the front. - Impact on Movement: Stays restricted bending and twisting but were essential for maintaining the fashionable silhouette of the time.

3. Petticoats - Layers and Fabric: Multiple petticoats made of wool, linen, or cotton were worn over the stays. Petticoats added volume to the skirt and provided warmth. - Tying Them On: Each petticoat was tied around the waist with tapes or drawstrings. This required reaching around the back, which could be tricky without assistance. - Impact on Movement: The multiple layers of petticoats made movement more cumbersome. They restricted leg movement, making it difficult to run or take large strides. Petticoats also added weight, making physical labor more exhausting.

4. Gown (Robe) - Style and Fabric: The gown, worn over the petticoats, was usually made from durable fabric like wool or coarse linen for servants. - Putting It On: The gown had a fitted bodice with a front-lacing or hook closure, which was easier to manage alone compared to back-lacing stays. - Full Skirt: The full skirt of the gown added another layer, further restricting movement.

5. Apron - Function and Appearance: An apron was worn over the gown to protect it from dirt and stains. It tied at the waist and sometimes had a bib that covered the bodice. - Tying the Apron: Tying the apron at the back was manageable but could still be awkward without practice.

6. Cap - Material and Design: A linen cap was worn to keep hair neat and clean. It was tied under the chin or at the back of the head. - Putting It On: This was relatively simple and could be done alone.

7. Shoes and Stockings - Shoes: Practical shoes made of sturdy leather with low heels or no heels were worn. Buckles or ties were used to secure them. - Stockings: Wool or cotton stockings were worn under the shoes and were held up with garters tied just below the knee. - Putting on Shoes: Bending down to put on shoes and tie garters was made more difficult by the restrictive stays and multiple layers of petticoats.

Challenges in Performing Chores¶

1. Carrying Things - Balancing Act: The full skirts and petticoats made it challenging to balance and carry large or heavy items. The layers added bulk, which could get in the way. - Stairs and Tight Spaces: Navigating stairs or narrow passages with full skirts required lifting the skirts slightly, often with one hand, making it difficult to carry objects with both hands.

2. Cleaning and Manual Labor - Bending and Reaching: Stays restricted bending and twisting, making tasks like scrubbing floors, reaching high shelves, or cleaning under furniture more challenging. - Sweeping and Dusting: Full skirts could get caught on furniture or knock over items while sweeping or dusting. - Laundry: Washing clothes involved a lot of bending and lifting heavy wet fabrics, which was cumbersome in stays and layers of clothing.

3. Cooking - Heat and Fire: Working near open flames while wearing flammable fabrics like linen and wool posed a fire hazard. Long skirts needed to be kept clear of the fire. - Mobility: Moving around a kitchen quickly, carrying pots and pans, and managing food preparation were all hindered by the restrictive clothing.

4. Outdoor Work - Weather: Heavy fabrics and multiple layers provided warmth but could become unbearably hot in warm weather. Conversely, wet weather could make clothing heavy and uncomfortable. - Footwear: Practical shoes protected feet but offered limited comfort and support for long hours on hard surfaces or rough terrain.

Conclusion¶

Dressing as a servant in the 1750s involved several layers of clothing that were difficult to manage alone, especially stays. The restrictive nature of stays and the bulk of petticoats and gowns made physical labor more challenging, requiring adjustments and careful movement to perform daily chores. These clothing challenges were a significant aspect of life for women, particularly for those in servitude.

If you have more specific questions or need further details, feel free to ask!

Robyn:

Thank you, that was useful. In France in the 1750s, how long were typical servant skirts, and did they typically wear more than one petticoat under those skirts? Also, how long were the chemises? Were the sleeves of the chemise visible on the arms when the gown was put on over the chemise? Were the stays visible at all?

Emily:

In 1750s France, the typical attire for female servants included several layers of clothing, each with its own length and visibility considerations. Here are the details:

Length and Layers of Servant Skirts and Petticoats¶

1. Skirts (Gowns) - Length: Servant skirts typically reached just above the ankles. This length was practical, allowing for easier movement and reducing the risk of tripping over the hem while working. - Material: Made from durable fabrics such as wool or coarse linen, these skirts were designed to withstand daily wear and tear.

2. Petticoats - Number of Petticoats: Servants commonly wore more than one petticoat. Two to three petticoats were typical, providing warmth and modesty without the excessive volume seen in higher-class fashion. - Length: Petticoats were generally the same length as the outer skirt, reaching just above the ankles. This ensured that they added fullness to the skirt without peeking out from underneath. - Material: Petticoats for servants were made from practical materials like wool, linen, or cotton, depending on the season and availability.

Chemises¶

1. Length of Chemises - Overall Length: Chemises typically reached mid-calf to ankle length. They were designed to be a comfortable undergarment that protected the outer clothing from sweat and body oils. - Sleeves: The sleeves of the chemise were long, usually reaching the wrists. They were often gathered at the wrists or elbow with a drawstring or simple cuff.

2. Visibility of Chemise Sleeves - Under the Gown: When a gown was worn over the chemise, the chemise sleeves were usually visible at the ends of the gown sleeves. This was because the sleeves of the gown were often slightly shorter or had open cuffs that allowed the chemise to show through. - Practicality: This design was practical, as it allowed the chemise sleeves to be easily laundered while protecting the outer gown sleeves from wear and tear.

Visibility of Stays¶

1. Under the Gown - Concealed: Stays were worn over the chemise but under the gown and petticoats, meaning they were not typically visible. The gown's bodice covered the stays completely. - Lacing: The lacing of the stays, whether front or back, was hidden by the gown, maintaining the appearance of a smooth, structured bodice.

Typical Attire for a Servant¶

  1. Chemise:
  2. Length: Mid-calf to ankle length, with long sleeves gathered at the wrist or elbow.

  3. Visibility: Sleeves visible at the ends of the gown sleeves, providing a neat and practical appearance.

  4. Stays:

  5. Support: Worn over the chemise to provide structure and support.

  6. Concealment: Fully covered by the gown, not visible.

  7. Petticoats:

  8. Number: Two to three petticoats for warmth and modesty.
  9. Length: Just above the ankles, same as the outer skirt.

2. Decorative Element - Ruffles and Trim: The upper edge of the chemise might be adorned with ruffles, lace, or other trims, adding a decorative border that framed the neckline and bust. - Function and Fashion: This visible part of the chemise served both a practical function, by protecting the outer gown from body oils and sweat, and a fashion function, by adding an elegant and feminine touch to the overall appearance.

This design allowed the chemise to act as a modesty layer while also enhancing the aesthetic appeal of the gown. - Modesty and Comfort: The chemise provided modesty, which was particularly important for servants and women of the lower classes, who needed to maintain a respectable appearance while working.

The gown would be made of sturdier fabric, designed for daily work. - Noblewoman’s Dress: For a noblewoman, the chemise might feature more elaborate lace and finer fabric, with the gown made of luxurious materials, showcasing the chemise as part of the overall ensemble.

The light-colored border you see in period images is indeed the chemise peeking out above the bodice of the gown. Robyn:

In challenges to performing chores, you mentioned 'outdoor work'. What sorts of outdoor work were FEMALE servants expected to perform as part of their duties?

Emily:

In the 18th century, female servants were often tasked with a variety of outdoor chores, depending on the household and the setting (urban vs. rural). Here are some common types of outdoor work that female servants might be expected to perform:

Types of Outdoor Work for Female Servants¶

1. Gardening and Yard Maintenance: - Kitchen Gardens: Tending to kitchen gardens was a common task. This included planting, weeding, watering, and harvesting vegetables, herbs, and fruits. - Flower Gardens: In more affluent households, female servants might also care for decorative flower gardens, including planting, pruning, and maintaining flower beds. - Lawn Care: Maintaining lawns and pathways, which included raking leaves, sweeping pathways, and occasionally mowing lawns (with scythes or similar tools).

2. Laundry: - Washing: Laundry was often done outdoors due to the need for large quantities of water. Female servants would wash clothes and linens by hand in large tubs or basins. - Drying: After washing, clothes and linens were hung out to dry on lines or laid out on bushes and hedges. - Beating and Whipping: Heavy items like rugs and blankets were often beaten to remove dust and dirt, a task usually performed outdoors.

3. Fetching Water: - Wells and Streams: In households without direct access to running water, female servants would fetch water from wells, streams, or public fountains. This involved carrying heavy buckets or jugs of water back to the house.

4. Gathering Fuel: - Firewood: Collecting and chopping firewood for cooking and heating was a necessary task. Female servants might gather smaller kindling and branches, while larger logs were typically handled by male servants. - Peat and Coal: In regions where peat or coal was used as fuel, servants might be responsible for gathering, storing, and transporting these materials.

5. Animal Care: - Poultry: Caring for chickens, ducks, and other poultry, which included feeding, cleaning coops, and collecting eggs. - Livestock: In rural households, female servants might help with feeding and milking cows, goats, and sheep, as well as cleaning stalls and barns.

6. Carrying and Transporting: - Market Visits: Female servants were often sent to the market to buy food and supplies. This involved carrying heavy baskets or bags back to the household. - Messages and Deliveries: They might also be tasked with delivering messages or small packages within the local area.

7. Cooking and Food Preparation: - Outdoor Cooking: In some cases, cooking tasks might extend outdoors, especially for larger households or during events. This included roasting meat over open fires or baking bread in outdoor ovens.

8. Cleaning and Maintenance: - Outdoor Cleaning: Sweeping courtyards, patios, and outdoor stairs, and keeping the exterior of the house tidy. - Outbuildings: Cleaning and maintaining outbuildings such as stables, barns, and storage sheds.

Challenges of Outdoor Work¶

1. Weather Conditions: - Rain and Mud: Working in wet conditions made tasks like fetching water or gardening more difficult and uncomfortable. - Heat and Cold: Extreme temperatures posed challenges, with heat causing exhaustion and cold making tasks like washing laundry painful and difficult.

2. Physical Demands: - Heavy Lifting: Many outdoor tasks involved heavy lifting, such as carrying water, firewood, or market goods. - Repetitive Strain: Repetitive actions, like scrubbing laundry or chopping wood, could lead to physical strain and injuries.

3. Clothing Restrictions: - Restrictive Clothing: The layered and restrictive nature of 18th-century clothing made bending, lifting, and moving more challenging. - Safety Concerns: Long skirts and petticoats could get caught or dirty, and stays restricted movement, making some tasks more hazardous.

By understanding these types of outdoor work and the associated challenges, you can provide a realistic depiction of the duties and daily life of female servants in the 18th century. If you have more specific questions or need further details, feel free to ask!

PAULINE takes TED’s hands and looks into his eyes.

PAULINE You can, and you will. It’s about survival, about adapting. And you’re not alone. I’m here to help you, to guide you. Together, we’ll make Dora as real and as strong as you need her to be.

TED looks at PAULINE, slowly nodding, beginning to accept his new reality.

This continuation allows Pauline to gently but firmly guide Ted into understanding the importance of fully embracing his new identity as Dora. Robyn:

G'day, Emily. I have a question about 1750s servant sleeping arrangements. Did female servants each have their own beds or did they often sleep in the same bed together?

Emily:

G'day, Robyn! In the 1750s, sleeping arrangements for female servants varied depending on the household and its resources. Here’s a detailed look at typical sleeping arrangements for female servants during that period:

Sleeping Arrangements for Female Servants¶

1. Shared Beds: - Common Practice: It was quite common for female servants to share beds. This practice was primarily due to economic reasons and limited space within servant quarters. - Benefits: Sharing a bed provided warmth during colder months, which was especially important in houses without modern heating systems.

2. Individual Beds: - Wealthier Households: In wealthier households with larger servant quarters, female servants might have their own individual beds. These households could afford more space and better accommodations for their staff. - Status and Role: Higher-ranking servants, such as housekeepers or ladies' maids, were more likely to have their own beds or even their own rooms, reflecting their status within the household hierarchy.

3. Dormitory-Style Rooms: - Servant Quarters: Many large households had designated servant quarters, often located in the attic or separate wings of the house. These quarters could resemble dormitory-style rooms with multiple beds. - Privacy: Privacy was limited in these arrangements, and personal space was often minimal. Curtains or screens might be used to create some semblance of privacy.

4. Conditions and Comfort: - Bed Quality: The quality of beds for servants was typically lower than that of the family members. Beds might be simple wooden frames with straw or feather mattresses. - Bedding: Servants often used basic linens and blankets. In less affluent households, bedding might be sparse and less comfortable.

Practical Considerations:¶

  • House Size and Layout: The size and layout of the house greatly influenced the sleeping arrangements. Smaller houses with fewer rooms would necessitate shared beds or communal sleeping areas.
  • Household Wealth: Wealthier households could provide better accommodations, including individual beds and more comfortable sleeping conditions.
  • Servant Hierarchy: The role and status of the servant played a significant role in determining their sleeping arrangements, with higher-ranking servants receiving better accommodations.

Conclusion:¶

In summary, it was quite common for female servants to share beds in the 1750s, especially in households with limited space and resources. Individual beds were more likely found in wealthier households or for higher-ranking servants. The conditions and comfort of these sleeping arrangements varied widely based on the household's wealth and the servant's role.

If you have more specific questions or need further details, feel free to ask!

In the 1750s, female servants typically wore simple and practical garments to bed. These garments were designed for comfort and modesty. Here’s a detailed look at what female servants wore to bed during that period:

Bedtime Attire for Female Servants¶

1. Nightgowns (Shifts/Chemises): - Primary Garment: The most common bedtime attire for female servants was a nightgown, often referred to as a shift or chemise. - Material: Made from linen or cotton, these garments were lightweight, breathable, and easy to launder. - Design: Nightgowns were loose-fitting and long, typically reaching to the ankles or mid-calf. They had long sleeves and a simple round or square neckline. Some might have drawstrings or ties at the neck and sleeves for adjustability.

2. Caps: - Night Caps: Female servants often wore linen or cotton caps to bed. These night caps helped keep their hair clean and contained, reducing tangles and maintaining neatness for the next day. - Design: Night caps were simple, usually with a gathered or pleated design, and sometimes tied under the chin.

3. Additional Layers: - Petticoats: In colder weather, servants might wear an extra petticoat or a woolen skirt for additional warmth. - Bed Jackets: Sometimes, a short bed jacket or shawl made of wool or flannel was worn over the nightgown for extra warmth.

Practical Considerations:¶

1. Modesty: - Design: The design of nightgowns emphasized modesty. They were loose and covered the body fully, which was important in shared sleeping arrangements where privacy was limited.

2. Comfort: - Material: Linen and cotton were chosen for their comfort and breathability, making them suitable for sleeping. - Fit: The loose fit of nightgowns allowed for ease of movement and comfort during sleep.

3. Warmth: - Layering: In colder months, additional layers like petticoats and bed jackets provided necessary warmth, as servant quarters were often less insulated than the main house.

Summary:¶

  • Nightgowns/Shifts/Chemises: Loose-fitting, ankle-length garments made from linen or cotton, serving as the primary sleepwear.
  • Night Caps: Simple caps worn to bed to keep hair neat and clean.
  • Additional Layers: Petticoats and bed jackets added for extra warmth in colder weather.

These practical and modest garments ensured that female servants were comfortable and appropriately dressed for the communal and often austere conditions of their sleeping quarters.

Robyn:

The chemise that was worn to bed was not the same one they wore under their stays during the day, was it?

Emily:

No, the chemise worn to bed was typically not the same one worn under their stays during the day. Here’s a detailed explanation:

Daytime Chemise vs. Nighttime Chemise¶

1. Daytime Chemise: - Purpose: The daytime chemise served as an undergarment worn beneath stays and other layers of clothing. Its primary function was to protect the outer garments from body oils, sweat, and dirt. - Material and Design: Made from linen or cotton, the daytime chemise was designed to be comfortable and breathable. It was usually long, reaching mid-calf to ankle length, with long sleeves and a simple neckline. - Durability: These chemises were sturdy and designed to withstand the friction and pressure from the stays and outer garments.

2. Nighttime Chemise (Nightgown): - Purpose: The nighttime chemise, often referred to as a nightgown or shift, was specifically for sleeping. It was designed for maximum comfort and ease of movement. - Material and Design: Like the daytime chemise, the nighttime chemise was made from lightweight linen or cotton. It was loose-fitting, long, and often featured long sleeves. The design was simple, prioritizing comfort over durability. - Cleanliness: Since hygiene practices required regular laundering, having a separate nightgown helped keep the daytime chemise clean for wearing under the stays.

Practical Considerations¶

  • Hygiene: Wearing a separate chemise for sleeping helped maintain better hygiene. The daytime chemise would absorb sweat and body oils during the day, while the nightgown could be laundered regularly to ensure cleanliness.
  • Comfort: The loose fit and simple design of the nightgown provided comfort during sleep, free from the restrictive layers worn during the day.
  • Preservation of Clothing: By having separate garments for day and night, the wear and tear on each piece of clothing was reduced, prolonging their lifespan.

Summary¶

  • Daytime Chemise: Worn under stays and outer garments during the day, made of durable linen or cotton, designed to protect outer clothing.
  • Nighttime Chemise/Nightgown: Worn exclusively for sleeping, made of the same materials but designed for comfort and cleanliness, separate from the daytime chemise.

By maintaining separate garments for day and night, female servants could ensure better hygiene and comfort, adhering to the practical and modest standards of the time. If you have any more questions or need further details, feel free to ask!
